“Certainly no such distinctive verse has appeared
among us for years.”— Sydney Morning Herald.
“ A collection of really admirable poems. There is no
mistaking the swing of his verses. Small wonder that
that such lines as these should stir the hearts of men. . .
We might go on quoting indefinitely.”— The Queens-
lander.
“A welcome addition to the scanty roll of writers of
distinctively Australian verse. The writer knows how to
tell a stirring incident in a spirited and vigorous maimer,
and the local coloring is exact and truthful. He is evi-
dently quite familiar with every aspect of bush and
station life.”— Australasian.
“The body of what lies between these covers is in some
respects distinctly better, as Australian literature, than
the few of Gordon’s poems in which the local element has
been made unmistakeable.”— Sydney Daily Telegraph.
“ The dominant tone is that of an intense affection and
longing for the wild, free, natural life of the country.”—

NOTES.
Two poems. This, by Brunton Stephens,
read at a Brisbane farewell concert to Gov.
Norman :
Three Words.
Hodson of Hodson’s Horse sat in his tent j
Over his table the warrior bent,
Pen in hand, telling his wife far away,
As was nightly his custom, the tale of the day ;
Telling the tale how the conflict had sped—
This one was wounded—and that one was dead—
Delhi unwon yet—our rulers at fault —
A woeful mistake to delay the assault—
And so on—all sad—till there flashed from his pen
Three words which, thank God, are as true now
as then —
Three words which, read now with their meaning
for US,
Take the pain from “Farewell” —for the record
runs thus,
“ Norman is safe.”
Thirty-eight years has the chronicle stood;
Thirty-eight years—yet the record holds good.
Let us cease from regret; let us rather rejoice
To hear even “ Good-bye ” from the actual voice
That brought cheer to the downcast and strength
to the spent
When Hodson, of Hodson’s Horse, wrote in his
tent,
“ Norman is safe.”
Thirty-eight years has the chronicle stood ;
Mbmy years still may the record hold good.
Arid such, even such, may the glad message be
That ere long shall be flashed to us under the sea—
The message which tells that in England at loot,
With his honors completed, his perils o’erpast,
“ Norman is safe.”
And this by Matthew Arnold :
The Progress of Poesy.
Youth rambles on life’s arid mount, .
And strikes the rock, and finds the vein,
And brings the water from the fount,
Tire fount which shall not flow again.
The man mature with labor chops
For the bright stream a channel grand.
And sees not that the sacred drops
Han off and vanish’d out of hand.
And then the old man totters nigh.
And feebly rakes among the stones.
The mount is mute, fhe channel dry ;
And down he lays his weary bones.
Gordon and Gotch supply “ Pears’ An-
nual,” one of the most magnificent of the
English pictorial annuals (Is. 3d. posted).
Three large coloured pictures accompany,
and the tale is a reprint of Dickens’s
“ Haunted Man ” —which, seen in this form,
suggests how far below the cockney genius
fall the nowadays providores of Christmas
numbers.
People have commented on the difference
between Mark Twain’s spoken and printed
humor. He hints the explanation himself,
in an article contributed to an American
paper. “ The humorous story,” says Mark,
“is American, the comic story is English,
the witty story is French. The humorous
story depends for effect upon the manner
of the telling ; the comic story and the witty
story upon the matter. The humorous story
bubbles gently along: the others burst.”
And Mark very obviously practises as he
preaches.
Mrs. Humphrey Ward’s new novel, “ Sir
George Tressady,” will be first published in
the Century magazine.
“ The Banjo’s ” book was issued by Angus
and Robertson on 19th October. By the
first week in November the publishers had
sold the entire edition, handed the author
his share of the profits, squared all accounts,
and were lying low for another literary
record-breaker.

“THE FAMILY AT MISRULE."
It is hardly a year since Ethel Turner’s
first book was published, and now she has
three to her credit—a trio of which many a
veteran author might be proud, let alone a
year-old dbbutante. It is true that other
years of toilsome apprenticeship preceded
this year’s freedom and success, when Miss
Turner has received her literary indentures
endorsed with the warm approval of every-
one, everywhere, competent to judge a
journeywomau in letters. The Australasian
public, at least, had learned to esteem her
talent in short stories, and their original
verdict has now found a world-wide echo.
It looks, indeed, as if we were on the eve
of a renascence of Australian literary art.
Since the era when our major bards and
prophets sang and saw—the era of Kendall,
and Gordon, and Stephens, and Clarke—
few of our writers have been able to give us
body as well as bouquet in their vintage.
“ Rolf Boldrewood,” beginning nobly, has
become a mechanical grinder of books
for the publishers. Mrs. Ward, Miss
“Keynotes” Dunne, and Marriott Wat-
son, are of us, but not with us, either
in heart or books. Mrs. Campbell
Praed, “ Ada Cambridge,” and “ Tasma ” —
the first much the most capable—merely
weave Australian scenery into the romances
of sentimental commonplace penned for their
feminine public. In “ Price Waning,”
Ernest Pavenc, Sydney Jephcott, Francis
Kenna, and two or three others, we have
had gleams of the fire that should light our
literary way. But not until quite lately—
perhaps not even yet—have we accumulated
a body of work which begins to compare
with that achieved by our early giants.
Now, with Louis Becke, Ethel Turner,
and “The Banjo’ well “arrived”; with
Lawson, and Daley, and Montgomery
approaching the goal ; with., many other
Bulletin writers well started in the race ; it
would really seem that 1900 will be able to
cull for a favorite book-shelf a dozen new
books of permanent value to take their place
beside the favorite old ones. The Bulletin
counts it indeed a sweet thing and honorable
that it has been permitted to assist in secur-
ing this happy consummation.
It is somewhat difficult to find words to
praise highly enough, yet truly enough, Miss
Turner’s newest book, “ The Family at
Misrule”—a sequel to “Seven Little Aus-
tralians.” The story is thoroughly feminine
—the bubbling over of a heart brink-full
with sympathy. It is feminine intuition
and feminine power of observation that
have aided the author to make her book
lovely and natural—so far as the children,
with one exception, are concerned. For
again the adults are not good : like Larrie
and Dot, they remain children in spite of
the formal labels and certificates of their
age. Again charm is the chief quality—
the charm of our contact with nerves all
a-tingle with tenderness. This phrase may
seem fantastic in cold print; but laying down
the book one does not feel it a whit too
warm. Only one Australian author since
Marcus Clarke—Henry Lawson—has had
such moving emotional art.
Yet the art, for the most part, is not in
the least artful. “ The Family at Misrule ”
is a book made in happy unison of heart and
head, with culture controlling but not
obtruding. Perhaps the most jarring note
of unreality is the'precocity of Poppet. Judy
at 13 was bad enough, but Poppet at 9 !
Well, fancy even a little Australian girl
capable of the complex emotion involved
when Poppet goes dramatically to defend
her disgraced brother before a schoolful of
boys, and the master hints that the naughty
one has told a lie before :—
(*)h, if only she could have flung back her head
and said, “ He has never told a lie in his life, sir;
never—never! ”
Shame at not being able to do so made the dear
curly head droop a little, and two more tears
forced their way from under her eyelids and fell
sadly down her cheek.
One flatly declines bo believe this of Miss
Poppet, at 9 ; though doubtless the pretty
sentiment would appeal to Miss Turner at
19, or later. And you can divide girlish
imagination from girlish observation all
through the book. Here, for example, is a
transcript from real life, exquisitely good
because absolutely true :—
In the nursery Meg was supposed to be giving
lessons to Peter and Poppet, and superintending
the more advanced studies of Nellie ; for the last
nursery governess had left suddenly, and the Cap-
tain had professed himself unable to afford another
until the next quarter.
Meg used to provide herself .with a book during
these daily struggles, to be indulged in at times
when her supervision was not required. It had
been an “improving ” book for the last month, for
she had lately been finding out how woefully
ignorant she was when she talked to .the young
man who had listened to her playing last night.
To-day it was Browning, because he had looked
horrified to find she never read any of his poems,
on the plea that he was acknowledged to be diffi-
cult to understand.
It was a pity she chose “Filippo Baldinucoi on
the Privilege of Burial” for her first essay, especi-
ally as it was such -a hot day ; but she had deter-
mined to read, dauntlessly, the first poem the
book open ad ait.
“ I)q this turn, Poppet,” she said, setting a
multiplication with eight fl <nires in e®eh line—
“ dear, what a greasy slate; and, Peter, if you
drop any blots on your copy, you will have to
write it again this afternoon.”
, Peter was sucking a little lump of ice he had
stolen out of the ice-chest. Poppet asked him for
a bit to clean her slate with, but he considered
this such waste of precious- material that he swal-
lowed it in a hurry and choked. Poppet asked if
she might go and wet her sponge ; but Meg said
no, it always took a quarter of an hour to do that
simple act, if she escaped from the room. So
Peter offered to breathe on it for her.
“Both of us will,” said Poppet—“you on the
top half, and me on the bottom. ”
Meg was taking a cursory glance at “ Filippo,”
and groaning mentally ; she did not hear the
arrangement for the slate-cleaning until the heads
bumped violently and the two began to quarrel.
“You licked it with your tongue,” Poppet said.
“ I never—I wath only breathing with my lipth
on it,” declared Peter.
“I saw the end of your tongue hanging out,”
Poppet maintained.
“You’re a thtory-teller, Poppet.” Peter’s face
began to get red. “ I wath only breathing, tho
there. ”
“ Peter, go and sit at the other end of the table.
Poppet, if you put out your tongue at Peter again
I shall make you stand in the corner.” Meg put
a pin in the Browning to keep it open, and went
over to Nell at the window to see how “ Le Ohien
du Capitaine ” was progressing.
“ Oh, Nell! ” she said.
The French dictionary lay face downwards on
the broad window-sill; “ Le Ohien ” was face up-
ward on Nell’s knee, but on the top of it was
“ Not Wisely, but too Well.”
“ Oh ! ” said Nell, with a gasp, her eyes misty,
her cheeks flushed —“ oh, it’s no use scolding, Meg
—I absolutely must finish this ; I’m just where
Kate is—Oh, Meg, you are horrid ! ”
For Meg had taken forcible possession of the
dark-green book, and had picked up the dic-
tionary.
“You know you are not to read in the morn-
ing,” sbe said; “ and I don’t think you ought to
read a love story like this till you’re eighteen at
least. Really, Nellie, it’s no use me pretending
to overlook you; you’ve done one page of “ The
Dog ” in three mornings. I’ll have to tell father
I must give up the pretence of teaching.”
“ Here, give it to me,” Nellie said, sighing wist-
fully; “it ought to be called ‘The Pig,’I think,
it’s so detestable. Put ‘ Not Wisely ’ on the table,
Miggie, so I can see the title and get occasional
refreshment.”
Then Meg returned to the “Privilege of Burial.”
This is as delightful as the burning of Mr.
Gillet is abominable. Mr. Gillet is a bush-
pathetic old lover of Meg’s who has to be
got out of the way to make room for a new
one ; and Miss Turner sends him up to
Heaven in a fiery cottage—with limelight,
slow music, and other stagey accessories which
draw an involuntary “ Ugh ! ” The scarlet-
fever episode passes with some difficulty,
but when the machine-god sets another
old pump working at our emotions one
thankfully turns back to Bunby and the
boys who cheer Poppet—not because she
championed a special favourite, but because
“ they felt it was expected of them, and it
was another break in the monotony ” of
morning school.
“ The Family at Misrule ” has many illus-
trations—without individuality or value—
and is published, with Miss Turner’s other
books, by Ward, Lock, and Bowden.
AN AUSTRALIAN BOYS’ BOOK.
Ernest Favenc’s new book for boys, “ The
Secret of the Australian Desert,” is a piece
of solid, conscientious work which no other
man could do so wisely or so well. For it
deals with the fate of lost Leichhardt ; pic-
tures his grave in the desert and the adven-
tures of his comrades ; and joins myth and
marvel, fact and fancy, in a style which
demands the knowledge of the explorer as
well as the imagination of the fictionist.
The book’s chief merit is that the whole
story might easily be true, yet it is as well
calculated to fascinate youthful readers as a
good story eminently false. There is interest,
too, for mature minds, in the glimpses of
the mysterious race which primevally
peopled Australia—and the sacrificial cave
ought to exist, if it does not :—
We took fire and bark and wood with us, and
when we emerged in the gloomy cavern beyond,
we immediately kindled a fire. As the blaze arose
and illuminated the recesses of the cave a shriek
of terror burst from my juvenile companions, a
wild cry of “ Waxlatta ! Warlatta ! ” and in an
instant they disappeared like a bevy of black rats
underneath the rock where we had entered. I
looked arounc * >■ >rprise, but soon divined the
cause; on the opposite side appeared, drawn in
white on the wall, a large triangle, the sign ever
associated in their minds with murder and rapine.
Heaping more ’wood on the fire, I advanced
and examined the surroundings. Underneath the
triangle was a huge block of yellowish-white sand-
stone, but its purity was marred by a horrible
reddish stain which marked one of its sloping
sides. Its purpose flashed on me at once—in
some olden time it had been used as a sacrificial
stone. The fire now blazed up merrily, and I had
ample light for my researches.
The smoke disappeared through crevices in the
roof, and the ventilation seemed excellent. Marks
of old fires were visible all over the floor .which
was of white sandstone, with the same recidish
stains visible in places. Searching more minutely
I found in one dbrner a knife or dagger of steel
(since then I have found it to be tempered so skil-
fully that the edge can scarcely be turned by the
hardest rock). The handle, if it ever Had one, had
disappeared through age. In addition, there
was a broken ring of the same metal, seemingly
part of a chain, and on the walls were characters
in red, but of no written language that I could
remember. This was all that I saw on my first
visit.
Voices at the opening told me that the
natives had recovered from their fright, and were
in search of me. I called to them, and emboldened
by my voice and the firelight some of them crept
in and joined me. I found out that they had no
knowledge of this chamber, and in hopes of
finding another I set them hunting round for
any more openings that might exist, but none
could be discovered. Whilst so engaged one of
them brushed against the stone altar, and immedi-
ately it commenced rocking, whilst a squeaking,
piercing scream, like a human being in intense
agony, thrilled us all with horror. The blacks
threw themselves on the ground, and it was a few
moments before I could summon up courage to
approach the stone and examine it. The rocking
was gradually ceasing, and the shrieks grew
fainter as the motion ceased. The stone I found
to be most beautifully poised, so that the slightest
touch started the oscillation. As to the machinery
that produced the screaming noise, that I could
not investigate without capsizing the stone, which
evidently weighed some tons. For a moment I
shut my eyes, and seemed to see once more the
hideous drama that must have been many times
enacted in this chamber of death—the savage
priests, the manacled victim, the streaming blood,
the trembling captives, and the harsh shrieking of
the rocking stone adding its awful voice to the
groans of the dying man and fading away into
silence with his last cries.
There is hardly blood enough in this passage
to give boys a fair idea of the treat in store for
them, but it is one which opensa sufficientview
of the author’s strength and style. One
notes with interest how unconsciously Mr.
Favenc takes the bushman’s view of rela-
tionships with the blacks. The white adven-
turers walk into blacks’ country and camps,
and are made welcome, afterwards attacking
and slaughtering their hosts without provo-
cation. And isn’t it characteristic, too,
when they come across a rocking-stone
which they know is a unique scientific,
marvel, that one of them should propose
shifting it with a plug of dynamite? On
another occasion, a black chief is sleep-
ing in their camp. Says one, “ Kick
old Columbus up.” And the author adds,
with delicate euphemism, “ Morton promptly
roused the slumbering chieftain.” Picture
the rousing process, and yon grasp at once
the attitude of white to black all over Aus-
tralia.
There is only one unrealistic passage in
Mr. Favenc’s story. He allows three sur-
vivors from Leichhardt’s expedition to live
few or many years among the blacks, but the
adventurers meet only one half-caste. Deli-
cacy might have subtracted that half-caste ;
truth would have multiplied him indefinitely.
Percy Spence contributes four admirable
illustrations, of quite unusual artistic value
for a boys’ book.

B L I IN P S.
The word is suggest! ve. Many dealers keep their
customers very much in the dark as to th'e true
state of things. So to speak, they get behind the>r
own blinds because the circumstances won’t allow
them to emerge into the full light.
OUR REPRESENTATIVE IN CHINA
writes as follows :
I should like to mention certain matters in connection
with the blind trade. With every order I place IiNSISI
ON INSPECTION before packing, with the result that
not less than 25 to 30 per cent, are rejected. But I find
this season a Ann has placed large orders, b'ut ABSO-
LUTELY 'NO INSPECTION takes place, consequently
makers are competing for the orders at lower rates on
these terms.
We take our friends behind the scenes so that
they may see WHAT A TRAGEDY the blind
trade is, and so that they may, when buying blinds,
discriminate between honest dealers and the other
sort. No doubt the blinds WHICH ARE
SHIPPED WITHOUT INSPECTION are
offered at lower prices than SELECTED BLINDS,
but wise people will prefer giving a trifle more,
should it be necessary, with the certainty of get-
ting BLINDS which offer some show of protec-
tion, and that do not tumble in pieces after a
fortnight’s wear.
BAMBOO BLINDS
made of the outside of skin or the bamboo in
its natural color. SPECIALLY SELECTED.
Fitted with CORDS and pulleys ready to put up.
It may be added that the CORDS are strong and
suitable, and not liable to rot and break on the
least provocation. Sizes from 3ft. wide by 6ft.
deep to 12ft. wide by Bft. deep ; prices, Is. to

Bards of the Backblocks.
MICK DOOLEY’S PANTS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
They brought a boy ffom Tallaran to run Mick
Dooley’s tracks ‘
They yarded him the fastest blood among the
fetation cracks,
With mdles and shirt and feloucher hat and pipe
with broken stem.
He slung into the saddle straight and waved his
hand to them.
The Sub, was lately out from “’Ome,” the
troopers both were green,
The tracking of an outlaw was a game they had
not seen;
This chippy little nigger and the antics that he
played—
They were rolling off their saddles at the funny
sight he made!
The tracker had a roving eye, he laughed a saucy
laugh,
He grinned as they were grinning, and he gave
them chaff for chaff;
The troopers both were solid men whose brains
had run to beef,
Bub when the boy got moving all their mirth was
turned to grief.
He was cautious ’mong the melon-holes, but when
the plain was sound
He led ’em at a gallop with his eyes upon the
ground.
And as odds are on a thoroughbred against a
trooper’s hacks
They were somewhat disconcerted at this mode of
running tracks.
He took ’em down the Flinders where the spear-
grass lined the brink,
Then crossed a stage of forty miles—without a
drop to drink,
And down the beds of dried-up creeks they
wandered all day long
Till life seemed, in a trooper’s view, one endless
billaboug.
Then turned he sharply to the west—the blue
M‘Ki'nlay Range,
And gave them joys of spinifex, in case they
wished a change;
And up and down the stony hills they tracked the
guilty Mick,
Except when they required a rest to be a little
sick.
They hauled their horses after them when hills
were tough and high,
And still the Sub. remarked “ Bai Jove;” his
eyeglass in his eye.
And still the blackboy pointed to the tracks which
he had seen,
Until they fairly bottomed—they had struck a
blind ravine !
Then one sharp-eyed suffering trooper gave a
grunt of savage joy
And called aloud unto the Sub. and pointed to the
boy :
“ The name upon them trousers ! Sure as God
made little ants—
Look, sir, this imp of Satan wears a pair of
Dooley’s pants.”
Like thought the tracker wheeled his mount and
vanished from their sight,
But as he thundered down the gorge he yelled
with all his might :
“ Mick Dooley’s crossed the Border now—no run
dat feller in—
Next time you want um tracker, boss—don’t yet
Mick Dooley's yin I ”
ESSEX EVANS.
AN AUSTRALIAN BIRD-SONG: THE
KOOKABURRA.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Millie May made the sunshine brighter
When her can she went bo fill,
Where the Bogan brook comes tumbling
Through the cleft in the granite hill.
Sandy Gray saw an ankle gleaming
As he ambled along that way—
“ Just the girl for an old man’s darling
tip there, mates ! What d’ye say ?”
Then the kookaburra laughing
Did to kookaburra call,
With a j.olly, jolly ku-ku, ku-ku,
Ku-ku, ku-ku, ku-ku, ku-ku, ha-ha-ah-ah,
Ha! ha ! ha !
Old Sandy came a-wooiug
To the hut by the Bogan gate,
Millie May was a damper mixing
As ho rode through the paddock gate;
The wise old bird came roosting
Oil a bough just overhead,
Millie looked up sly and wicked,
And just guess what the old bird said—
“ Sure old fools will be still fools,
Now heed what may befall,
Witli a jolly, jolly, &c.
“Mr. Gray, oh my, you’re welcome,
Mr. Gray, will you please sit down ?
Sure, mother she’s off to the station,
And the old man’s gone to town.”
Then old Sandy’s beard went wagging,
And the old bird laughed right out,
And every mate who heard him
Came roosting round about.
But never a sound they uttered,
Twas a bird in the bush let fall
His distant ku-ku, &c.
Old Sandy he meant business,
And made the running fast—
“Oh, Millie, Millie May, I love you,
And I’ve got it out at last.”
Millie May didn’t wait to answer,
But she made him understand—
And not with a kiss bub a sounding smack
Of a right strong floury hand.
And was ever there heard such laughter
As these sad old birds made all,
With their jolly, jolly ku-ku ku-ku, &c.

IMPORTANT NOTICE TO CIGARETTE SMOKERS.
WE teg to inform our customers and cigarette smokers generally that the American Tobacco Co,,
Ltd., of N.S.W., who control the sale of cigarettes known under the brands of “ Allen and
Ginter’s Straight-cut No. 1,” “Cameo” and “ Old Judge,” and who manufacture the colonial-made
cigarettes called “ Pure Gold ” and “ Dixon’s No. I,’’ have given us official notice that they will not
supply us with any of their American-made cigarettes unless we agree to relinquish the agency held by
us for New South Wales for the world-renowned English firm of W. D. and H. O. WILLS, Limited,
the manufacturers of those popular high-class cigarettes known as
“ THIIR-lEIE CASTLES” and “ C-A-IPST-A-HST.”
We have declined to conform with this request, as we feel it would not be to the nterest of
cigarette smokeis in New South Wales to allow the American Tobacco Co.. Limited, to monopolise
the whole of the cigarette trade. We, therefore, appeal to cigarette smokers to try the celebrated
Wills’ “Three Castles” and “Capstan” Cigarettes,
And compare their quality with that of any other. We are confident that their verdict will be in
favor of the
“Three Castles” and “Capstan” Cigarettes,
And by smoking these they will prevent any one Company from attempting to dictate what people
should or should not smoke.
HEYDE, TODMAN & CO. Agents for W. D. and H. 0. WILLS, Ltd.).

“ YANKEE DOODLE” is everywhere spoken of as the finest plug-tobacco that has evei
appeared here, and it has won greater success during the short time it has been on the market than
anything previously. This is no idle boast, and, to prove it, any smoker can test the statement for
there are few towns in N.S.W. where “YANKEE DOODLE” is not known and sold. Its’sale
has this year increased nearly one hundred (100) per cent over that of last year. And why is this?
Simply that “YANKEE DOODLE” has struck the popular taste. In the first place, it comes, after
years ,of careful study, from a combination of certain choice Virginian tobaccos, so blended apd
exquisitely flavoured, that any smoker who can stand a tobacco heavier than the pure gold-leaf is
forced to admit that he has at last discovered something far and away better than anything he has
been accustomed to, DIXSON & SONS have taken over half-a-century to accomplish this; and as
their leading business has been built up on their reputation for QUALITY, they could not afford to
make the foregoing statement if any doubt existed. DIXSON & SONS, who use more American leaf
than all the other Tobacco Factories combined, are noted for several high-class brands* “OON
QUEROR,” “SIRROGER,” “CHAMPION,” and “DOLLAR;” but “YANKEE DOODLE*
ranks supreme.

SATURDAY, NOVEMBER 23, 1896.
The Unemployed.
Once a year or so the Labor-party moves
the adjournment of N.S.W. Assembly to call
attention to the necessity of doing “some-
thing ” for the unemployed, and, its regular
time having arrived, the regular motion came
along last week and was discussed in the
regular desultory fashion. Nothing in par-
ticular ensued, save the high-toned contempt
of the Superior Person who edits the daily
paper. When Premier Reid was merely
Leader of the Opposition, one of his bitterest
accusations against the then Premier Dibbs
was that the latter politician hadn’t solved
the unemployed problem, and one of the
reasons why Reid was put in Dibbs’,s place
was his emphatic promise that when he
attained to power he would find the long-
sought-for solution. He has been in power
for a considerable time, and the only change
so far is that he has employed a few of the
workless multitude at forest-thinning and
sand-shifting with borrowed money, whereas
his predecessor sent a few of them out fos-
sicking without the borrowed money. In
each case, however, the relief made no
visible impression on the great mass of
trouble. Premier Reid also promises to
borrow more money and set a few more of
the unemployed to making roads and
bridges, which is the same remedy that
his predecessors have adopted for thirty
years past with no good results. And
when, in a few months, the borrowed
money is spent, the temporary laborers will
be discharged, and things will be just where
they were, except that the addition to the
public debt will have made them a little
worse than before.
• • #
In short, Premier Reid t promises to do
something large and statesmanlike and really
effective for the unemployed only brand him
once more as a windy humbug. He has
nothing new to propose ; his relief works
and his borrowed money are only the same
old futile passing of the responsibility on to
posterity which has been the policy of all his
predecessors. Parliament also has nothing
definite to suggest. The Labor-party which
persistently urges that “something ’ shall
be done fails to define that something in any
useful fashion ; it only beats the air and
demands in a hazy fashion that a person not
defined shall rise up and do a thing not very
well specified. And the daily press sneers
in a lofty fashion, on the ground that
there always have been more or less of the
“ unemployed,” and therefore there always
will be, and the man who talks about
abolishing this evil is a fool and a dreamer.
This, at least, is wholly illogical. There
always has been crime, yet the world
goes on trying to suppress it by the same
devices which have never been a complete
success for 6000 years or more. There
always has been disease, and the world
keeps pottering along manfully in search of
new remedies. There always, so far as is
known, have been drink and immorality and
dirt and bad language and ugliness and want
of religious sentiment and war and many
other unpleasant things, and man keeps on
trying to effect small improvements in all
these directions and yet makes very slow
progress. And if mankind is to let go the
great problem of poverty because it looks so
large and hopeless, then it should let all the
others go also for the same reason, and
allow the world to slide.
* * m
Among all the uncertainty of the unem-
ployed problem a few things are still reason-
ably sure. Nothing can be done in any
one province by laying down the axiom
suggested by the N.S.W. Labor-party that
it is the duty of the State to find work for all
who need it ; to do so would only mean that
that particular province would have to find
work for all the unemployed of Australasia.
An attempt was recently made to embody
the same axiom in the Swiss constitu-
tion, but it was promptly rejected by
popular vote on the ground that its
adoption would presently compel Switzer-
land to find work for all the unemployed of
Europe. When Australia is federated, and
the law rigidly excludes from its shores the
poverty-stricken aliens of Asia and the worst
class of European pauperism, then the State
may assume the responsibility on certain
conditions. The work supplied must be paid
for out of revenue ; to create work with
borrowed money only means that the evil is
postponed, not abolished, and that when
borrowing ceases, either through the col-
lapse of the money-market or through in-
ability to pay more interest, the unemployed
evil will be worse and more utterly hopeless
than before. Also, the work must be of a
sort that will yield revenue, either directly
or indirectly, else it will be a mere system
of pauper relief, and the taxation of the
community to relieve poverty will create
more poverty, and the latter end of the
country will be worse than the beginning.
Moreover, it must be recognised that the
“finding” of work for the unemployed is,
at the best, a mere surface remedy and
doesn’t touch the actual disease. In a
healthy and well-organised community work
should not require “finding”—it should
exist in the natural course of things. But,
meanwhile, k.,is necessary to deal with
things as they are, and to find the best
remedy that can. be devised without handing
down the trouble with compound interest in
the future.
* * *
One of the suggestions advanced by the
Labor-party is that the State should con-
struct public works with money raised by
the issue of a local paper-currency. The
ordinary note-circulation of New South
Wales is about £1,500,000, and a gold
reserve of £400,000 would be far more than
sufficient to keep the circulation afloat.
This would leave the State £1,100,000 as a
permanent loan. Therefore it follows that,
if this amount was invested in railways
which yielded even per cent, on the
cost of construction, it would mean a
considerable amount of employment in the
immediate present and—after allowing for
the maintenance of the pape.r-currency—a
steady revenue of about per annum
to the Treasury. But the assumption that
this would be all profit is unfortunately
erroneous. The Treasury, under the latest
Banking Act, makes no less than £33,000 a
year by taxing the circulation of the private
banks, so the amount to be gained by this
change of system is not so large as it appears.
At the best, the State would get a loan of
£1,100,000 per annum at a cost in revenue
and working expenses of some £35,000 a
year. The principal object of a State-note
issue is the security of the currency, but the
actual profit to be derived from this single
branch of the State Bipik would be very
limited, for a time, at all events,
• *
Beyond this there is another possible ex-
pedient for raising money also advocated in
certain quarters—a forced paper currency.
There is no doubt that New South Wales
could carry a certain amount of forced
currency-possibly as much as £1,500,000
without depreciation ; only two years ago it
carried a-considerably larger amount at° the
most disastrous period of its history with no
visible ill-effects. Under this system, no
gold-reserve would be required, and the
whole £1,500,000 would be available. The
hackneyed argument that in Russia and
some states of South and Central America,
where the system has been carried to
ridiculous excess, and solely because of that
excess, it has resulted in a very heavy fall in
values proves nothing. In Central America,
the principle of popular suffrage, instead of
producing political liberty, has got strangely
mixed up with revolutions, riots, bloodshed
and military dictatorships, but no such
results have followed in Australia, and the
one illustration is quite as much to the point
as the other. The real objections to the
forced paper currency idea in this case are
that it is only a temporary and insufficient
expedient, and that, as it would almost
inevitably drive the ordinary bank-notes out
of circulation, the large annual revenue at
present derived from the note tax would
be lost, and a profitable branch of the
business of the future State Bank wholly
destroyed. Therefore, the State would be
simply raising money in the present by
sacrificing revenue in the future, which is so
much akin to the old device of passing the
trouble on to posterity that it has very little
to recommend it. And, in any case, the un-
employed trouble has grown to such dimen-
sions that a much larger measure of relief is
wanted than can be obtained from any paper
currency system.
• mm
Another and more permanent form of
relief, which so far doesn’t seem to be advo-
cated by any political party, is the simple
and straightforward method of raising another
£500,000 per annum by taxation, or saving
£500,000 per annum out of expenditure, or
doing both at once, and spending the pro-
ceeds in public works. There are many
railways still wanting to be built in New
South Wales which might easily pay 1 per
cent, on their cost, but which certainly
cannot pay per cent, for many years to
come, and therefore cannot be built with
borrowed money without becoming a heavy
burden on the Treasury. The possibility of
saving £500.000 per annum out of expendi-
ture is sufficiently evidenced by the enor-
mous cost of governing New South Wales
as compared with the adjacent province of
Victoria. The possibility of raising another
£500,000 of revenue without unduly distress-
ing the community is demonstrated by the
fact that New South Wales is now much
more lightly taxed than Queensland, Maori-
land, Westralia, Tasmania, Victoria, or Great
Britain, and, in proportion to the alleged
wealth of the inhabitants, it is almost—if not
quite—the most lightly-taxed country upon
earth. If £1,000,000 per annum were raised
in the manner suggested, and spent upon
necessary public works, one-half of which
yielded no direct revenue while the other
half returned only 1 per cent., it would
solve the unemployed problem in a great
measure for the present, and would, in 20
years, even if the returns did not advance
beyond the preliminary 1 per cent., supply
a revenue of £lOO,OOO per annum. It would
also do more or less indirect good to the
country, according to the intelligence with
which the money was invested. There is
absolutely no reason why this expedient
should not be adopted, save that peculiar
kink in the character of the cornstalk in-
habitant, which makes him boast his country
to be the richest on earth, while, at the same
time, he declares its utter inability to sup-
port a burden of taxation which much poorer
communities endure with apparent ease.
• # *
All this has no direct bearing on the aboli-
tion of the unemployed difficulty, and even
the expenditure of £1,000,000 a year on
public works would, at the best, be only a
palliative. But, at the worst, it would be a
palliative—it would relieve much distress in
the immediate present, and add a great deal
to the aggregate wealth of the country in
the future without adding to its permanent
burdens, and it would supply some revenue,
however little, whereas the relief that is
gained by the expenditure of borrowed
money only supplies new liabilities. The
causes of the unemployed trouble are largely
the uneven distribution of wealth, and to
alleviate this evil, especially under a cum-
brous political system which seems specially
created to prevent anything being accom-
plished, is a work of much time and diffi-
culty. Meanwhile it is necessary to do
something, and shifting the burden on
to posterity by raising foreign loans is not
doing anything. The unemployed problem
can’t be solved by mere theories, nor by any
process which will cost nothing, nor can it
be left alone on the false and foolish theory
that it is the natural order of things and a
part of the great scheme of creation, nor can
it be honestly passed on to future genera-
tions. All the methods yet devised for
immediate relief involve the expenditure of
much money, and the only sane process is to
raise that money by increasing revenue and
redueing expenditure, and invest the amount
thus saved to the best advantage.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Governor Brassey and “The Age.”
The grovel-rash apparent on the surface of
Victoria for some weeks past is a good deal
owing to its “ radical ” daily print. All such
skin diseases originate in -the press, and are
more ©r less epidemic, according to the amount
of space devoted to “ loyalty ’’-germ culture by
Eenny news-sheets. And until the average
uman being shall have quite eradicated the
ungodlike instinct of snobbery from Ids nature,
the worship of new Governors will be en-
couraged by what is called “ respectable jour-
nalism.” In the x ordinary way of business
Melbourne’s Argus and ’ Age, for instance,
must, both of them, grovel to live. On the
other hand, they are not compelled to live to
grovel, and Democracy asks of a journal alleged
to be democratic that it will at times, when
the aforesaid grovel-rash is unavoidable, do as
little as possible to circulate the loathsome
infection. One scarcely expects this moderation
In evil-doing of a Tory print, like the Arguß.
which has a political licence to take its Lord
Brassey very seriously, and a standing order
from sassiety to exaggerate his Excellency’s
public and private importance, If ah ArHuß
choose to lick the new Governor’s boots its self-
aba'sement is not out of harmony With “ loyal ”
fanaticism, whereas a radical editor is not en-
titled to do more, under the worst of Circum-
stances, than pat Brassey’s vice-regal back.
J herefore, the more reasonable portion of
Melbourne has been astounded to find
the Conservative flunkey paying some respect
to common sense, whilst the Age lay prostrate
at Lord B.’s feet, caressing his ,’Ome-made
uppers with its staunch Protectionist tongue.
In recording the Governor’s arrival, the “ Radi-
cal reporters out-A roused their brethren on
the rival publication. From day to day the
Age has maintained this disgusting-,supremacy
in idolising the golden calf. Last week Brassey
presided over a free-and-easy concert at the
Seamen s Institute, Port Melbourne. A
plain, 20-line paragraph notice of the puny
event sufficed for the Argus, but the
Age broke out into another column of con-
tagious inflammation. In a full -and par-
ticular account of all that the new Governor
said and did at the little nautical corroboree,
the rag actually gloated over an incident which
it should have deplored. A seaman, named
Wild, sang a song. Here is the Age’s com-
ment on his vocal effort ; “ Mr. JVild i s a
member of the Sunbeam’s crew, and Lord
Brassey, in announcing him, pleasantly re-
marked, ‘ Mr. Wild and I have swept the seas
together.’ At the conclusion of his"song, and
as he was returning to his seat. Lord Brassey
leaned over to address him privately. ' 4 The sea-
faring members of the audience were routed to
cheer his • Excellency's action in conversing with
one of his crew , and as the noise thus made pre-
cluded the continuance of the conversation,
Lord Brassey smilingly remarked, ‘ Ah, I
thought I might have been permitted a small
exchange of confidence with Mr. Wild.’ A
paper pretending to champion the masses i\ the
classes doesn’t, as a rule, publish a large
quantity of that sort of stuff within three
weeks unless something has occurred to change
its entire frame of mind. The Bulletin
begins to fancy that Governor Brassey has
bought a half-share in the Age.
The Flogging- Superstition,
Some of the casual doings of last holiday
deserve to be thought over very hard by the
legislators of New South Wales when the next
Tlogging Bill comes up for consideration.
Whenever the necessity to do something for
the suppression of the larrikin curse becomes
more than usually apparent some breezy legis-
lator of tbe R. L. O’Connor species conies for-
ward with a Bill for the copious flogging of the
local rowdy—when he is caught—on the alleged
ground that the present laws are not sufficient
to deal with the evil. The real trouble,
according to all thinking people, is that the
present laws haven’t even had a trial. There
are not half enough policemen to find and run
in the larrikin to begin with, and the natural
consequence is that in about 99 per cent, of
cases he isn t captured, and if he can’t be caught
it really doesn’t matter what awful things
might happen to him if he was caught. And
on the very rare occasions when he is arrested
and a particularly bad case is proved against him,
a weak-kneed magistracy, instead of givingdiim
the full term in gaol, or committing him for trial
so that he may get a spell of penal servitude,
generally fines him 40s. On the recent holiday,
the Sydney “push” took possession of Manly
bodily and held a reign of terror. In the princi-
pal street alone about a dozen bloodthirsty fights
took place on a large scale, and, after one of
them, three insensible wrecks were left on the
pavement. The worst case had his face almost
destroyed through being kicked in and his
features trampled on, and when the push had
moved on, and was starling a new fight with
beer-bottles further down the street, he was
removed to the hospital. The other two
merely had their heads somewhat jumped upon,
and were dragged into a quiet spot to revive.
Similar scenes went on all day, and the scanty
police force, as a rule, didn’t turn up, either
because it was too much engaged elsewhere or
else because the odds were a great deal too
heavy ; and no reinforcements of any value
arrived from the city. Not one of the real
rioters was arrested, and, considering that they
were several hundred strong, all the flogging-
laws in creation wouldn’t have made their
arrest any more practicable. Even if R. E.
O’Connor devised a statute for boiling tbe
obnoxious rowdy alive, when he is caught, it
would make no difference so long as
the law isn’t strong enough to catch
him, or doesn’t dare to put the sta-
tutes in operation on the rare occa-
sions when an arrest is effected. Under
present conditions the stereotyped legislators
whose one idea of suppressing disorder is to
enact that something awful will happen when
they get hold of the criminal are just as ridicu-
lously futile as an inebriated aboriginal in the
back-blocks who is going to kill the white man
Government with a waddy when he captures it.
What is wanted first of all is a police-force
strong enough to arrest the brutal larrikin with
reasonable regularity and a magistracy that has
pluck enough to enforce the existing laws
when he is arrested. It is time enough to
think of fixing up new deterrents when the old
deterrents have had a trial.
Vomited Slightly.
The Boodlewraith policy is nauseous, but Q.
Parliamentary majority swallows it, for the
most part, without a wry face. The strongest
stomach, however, has its queasy turns, and
occasionally the Government proffers a dose
which even the Government’s undiscriminatiim
supporters reject. Such- was the-M-rranr ratri
way, which the beaten Ministry withdrew last
week with ill-concealed disgust. Queensland
heavily-taxed, with a huge deficit just con-
verted into funded debt, is doubtfully in
a position to build railways at all. If
she be, it is certain that she is not
in a position to construct more unprofit-
able lines. I his was so far recognised that


Parliament a couple of months ago passed
a Railways Guarantee Act, providing that a
reasonable return from new lines must be guar-
anteed, in case of deficit, by the people of the
districts through which they pass. The Mirani
line—a 25-mile extension of the existing Mackay
railway—had no public recommendation what-
ever. It is certain that it could not pay :
the railway commissioner says so, local
residents say so, everybody says so. There
is no population; nothing to yield freight;
little land of value about its course. But the
Mirani line had several strong Cabinet recom-
mendations. It passed through selection after
selection which Ministerial politicians owned or
were interested in. It furnished an admirable
bribe for the district which returns to Parlia-
ment Minister Dalrymple and Ministerial-
Advocate Ohataway. It promised to supply a
quite exceptional opportunity for patronage and
jobbery—two things which the unlucky neces-
sity for retrenchment has lately tended un-
pleasantly to lessen. So Boodlewraith fought
for it tooth and nail, wept for it, raved for it,
stonewalled for it, threatened for it. But the
scandal was too great, the job was too gross,
and the Ministerial whip could not round up
the tail at division-time. So Labor, Opposition,
and honest politics scored a victory for once—■
to their own exceeding surprise. But the inci-
dent is too rare to have prophetic value. A
worm may turn —and its stomach —but it can
never rise and stand erect. And the Queens-
land Parliamentary variety crawls far too
naturally to try.
More “ Meejum.”
Editor Bulletin, Sir —ln your issue of
August the 17th there is a friendly criticism of
bimetallism. You recognise, in the case of
wheat, that, because of the fall in silver, the
Indian fanner who exports to England gets
20 florins for a sovereign, whereas your own
exporting farmer only gets 10 florins—hence
the advantage enjoyed by the one over the
other. But you proceed to point out that, as
with the rupee, so with the depreciated peso
paper of Argentina ; and you query whether,
in the case of depreciated paper currencies, the
restoration of silver internationally would do
any good. Mr. Brett, who farms 40,000
acres in the Plate, in giving evidence before
the Royal Commission on Agriculture, now
sitting, having pointed out that, with a gold
premium of 250 per cent., the Argentine wheat
exporter got seventy shillings of his paper-
money for one sovereign, declared also that he
was paying no higher wages than when gold
was at par with paper ; and, further, that the
purchasing power of the paper peso in Argen-
tina is very little less than before. The unfair
and unnatural stimulus to exports of produce
from countries with depreciated currencies,
whether silver or paper, has ruined not only
our farmers, but also the export trades of your
seven colonies. The restoration of silver at its
old rate, 1 to 15J, v/ould evidently be effective
in cancelling the present bonus on exports
from India, China, and Japan, but your
point is—an all important point—would it
also assist us in the case of the com-
petition of Argentina and Russia? The
opinion of bimetallists is that it would ; that
gold premiums are the recognition of the
diminution of the volume of international 1 g I
tender money; restore that volume to its
normal dimensions by restoring silver, and gold
premiums will disappear. It may perhaps be
replied, “Oh, but if depreciated paper both
stimulates exports and operates as an automatic
tariff to contract imports, then debtor nations
like Russia and Argentina will again pump
additional paper-money into their circulation
even if bimetallism by Europe has brought
their paper-currency to par.” But this is not,
I think, a sound argument. Statesmen and
financiers in both countries deplore the depre-
ciation of their paper. While recognising
that it expands their exports and enables
them to pay their foreign debts which
must otherwise be in default, yet they
are aware that it hampers their domestic
exchanges and restricts their foreign credit.
But, apart from the wheat industry, look at
your tin production, which at one time promised
to show a rapid expansion. How can you work
your tin mines when there is apparently no
limit to the tonnage which Singapore can
supply from Pahang for 650 of those silver
dollars with which she pays her workers ; that
is to say, for £65 per ton, with the dollar at
10 to the sovereign, for £l3O with the dollar at
20 to the sovereign. Or look again at your
Newcastle coal-trade; Japan can supply the
Far East with unlimited coal, f.0.b., for 4 yen
per ton ; for 16s. with the yen at its old rate of
gold exchange, but for Bs. with the yen shrunk
to one half. One more illustration, which will
amuse you. Mr. Kopsch, the secretary to the
Imperial Board of Maritime Customs at
Shanghai, has pointed out to me in a recent
letter that formerly the export of English
umbrellas to China was immense; the 15s.
umbrella was retailed for dollars. When,
because of the fall of silver, the importers had
been obliged to charge seven dollars, that price
was found to be prohibitive, and at this point
Japan took up the tale, sending China a splendid
imitation of the English gingham for four
dollars. Japan has now succeeded in securing
the entire trade, and is exporting a thousand
umbrellas daily ; 362,000 for the year 1894. It
is safe to say that in the export umbrella trade
alone, at least £5OO per day, formerly paid in
wages to white men, is now being paid, because
of the fall in silver, to yellow men. Mr.
Kopsch also points out that in consequence of the
fall in her exchanges, China last year for the
first time was able to send wool to Europe, her
wool export for the year aggregating 270,000
cwts. There is no trade employing white
labor safe, if white money is to be cheapened
further by the legislation of the creditor and
bond-holding classes in the West; and happily,
even 1 though rather late in the day, our working
classes here have awakened to the fact that
cheap silver involves an unnatural and unneces-
sary competition, which is resulting in cheap
men and women.—Yours faithfully, Moreton
Prewen. Hopetoun House, South Queens-
ferry, Scotland, Oct. 1, ’95.
The White Knight and White Slavery.
Melb. Metropolitan Board of Fitz Gibbon con-
tinues its Tory methods. While he was
dedicating a slab of slaver in alleged rhyme to
the bounder Baron, one of his Commissioners
was attacking the methods of the Board :
Councillor M'Mahon (Fitzroy) said it was no use dis-
guising the fact that the minimum-wage question, as
regarded the contracts of the Metropolitan Board of
Works, was absolutely a farce. If a contractor was
detected he was fined, and the amount of fine went into
the coffers of the Board, instead of the man who had
been wronged. It had been proved that a young man, 21
years of age, with a wife and child to support, had been
working for 12s. 6d. per week at 12 hours per day, and
only 20 minutes allowed for meals.
The cynical encouragement given by Fitz-
Gibbon to this sort of thing, despite the
popular clamor against sweating, exists because
the Board consists of Fat Men, the nominees of
Fat Man, and some weak followers of Fat Man
—the Democratic minority being negligible.
The average commissioner is appointed because
he gives good dinners, or because he was dis-
appointed in not being elected mayor. Whether
he has the confidence of the ratepayers in
spending their money is not considered. He
takes his seat on the Board, and is duly
nobbled by the White Knight. The strange
thing about the Gibbon is that when he is most
in earnest and most honestly acting up to his
convictions, the interests of the Democracy are
in the greatest danger. His eloquence, his
bombast, his attorney-like cunning, are always
arrayed on the side of his honest convic-
tions, and generally against the public interest.
This suits the average commissioner right up to
his thick head—he likes to be on the side of the
pious man who howls for Rule Britannia and
damns the mere public. Sometimes, of course,
there is a small majority against FitzGibbon—
they get their resolution on the minutes and
Fitz Gibbon gets his will into fact. He is
absolute master and dominant lord over the
stomachs of his Bored, and the sooner he is
stopped making industrial war with a light
heart, the better. The remedy is direct
responsibility of the commissioners to the people
they are supposed to represent.
The Much-worried Miner.
N.S.W. Mines Minister Smith, whose thoughts
run easily to conferences, is now calling to-
gether representatives of the miners of the pro-
vince. The wardens are being instructed to
convene meetings in their districts, and these
meetings (which will probably be attended by
about one-tenth of the mining population) will
name the representatives. Just what the
Minister proposes that the conference shall do
is not clear. If he wants a damning criticism
of pretty well the whole Mines administration,
that should be easily enough got. Firstly, the
mining regulations, which are supposed to be
the miners’ good guardian angel, and conse-
quently are ordered to be set aloft at every
mine, need a thorough overhaul ; for they
rarely stand when they are brought before a
Judge. So thoroughly afraid are mining inspec-
tors of these precious regulations that they never
by any thing but a great mischance bringanaction
against the most erring of managers if he is of
a fighting sort. Take one regulation, which
very trustful miners are in the habit of pro-
nouncing with great emphasis—to the effect
that the occurrence of an accident shall be
■prima-facie evidence of neglect on the part of
the mine-owner. That was brought before Mr.
Justice Stephen in a case at Broken Hill.
“ Nonsense,” was his Honor’s remark, as he put
it aside. Then in another case—or was it the
same?—the report of the Inspector of Mines
was tendered as evidence; and his Honor
wouldn’t admit it. Then, if the regulations
are quite unreliable, so are the officers.
Example : —A miner was putting in some shots
at a Broken Hill mine. He had fired
two, and was lighting the third when
the first hole exploded. Though terribly
knocked about, he crawled away from the
face. Another miner, working some yards
away, told the boss of the accident, and appar-
ently glorified himself. The story went to the
Minister for Mines, and from the Department a
letter issued in the course of a couple of
months conveying to miner No. 2 the Minister’s
thanks for his heroic action. Then the heroic
miner’s mates came along to say that the whole
story is—well, incorrect, and make it plain that
the Minister and the Chief-Inspector are quite
prepared to act on the flimsiest ex-parte state-
ment. But that was a small blunder, though
annoying enough to the men who knew the
facts. A far more serious one was the letting
of the opal-bearing ground at White Cliffs in
huge mineral leases —a system that has led to
heart-breaking tributing. Then there is that
great way-back question of tanks on mining
fields—tanks which, somehow, the squatter
always seems to get hold of sooner or later.
There is six months’ work for a conference
here ; after that there is the prospecting-vote
question to handle; and what illustrative stories
could be told when the business comes on !
And before the conference breaks up, after hav-
ing said a word or two about a permanent
mining-accident fund, the subject of the fees
collected by mining registrars might be profit-
ably handled. What is the matter with the
Mines Department is that it has been dominated
most of the time by men who (like Minister
Smith) know nothing whatever of mining, and
who have listened to men who confound mining
and stock-jobbing and “first-robbering,” and to
theorists, some of whom should have known
better. The absurd importance with which
mining on private lands was viewed illustrates
this. One would have thought, if one had
judged by the speeches, that every unemployed
man in the province would get work and pocket-
fuls of gold if only there was a Mining on
Private Lands Act. Such an Act was passed
a poor one, it may be—and then the people who
had been impressively talking of millions of
acres of rich gold-bearing country mysteriously
disappeared.
A Side Issue.
The Green problem is one of the small side
mysteries of the Dean conspiracy case. Daniel
Green, a person of no great importance, was
arrested on a charge of conspiring with Crick
and Meagher to defeat the ends of something
r other. He was arrested again on a charge
of conspiracy in another case, and remanded
persistently for many weeks until at last the
“ Crown ” dropped the proceedings and he was
discharged. He was further arrested on a
charge of brutally assaulting a female witness,
and on this charge also he was remanded on the
application of the “ Crown ” for many weeks
without a hearing. When at last the miser-
able case came on it proved that he had been
arrested on a description so utterly unlike him
that no sane policeman could have mistaken
him for the individual wanted. Even the
arresting constable in his evidence acknowledged
the discrepancy. Further, it transpired that,
at the time the alleged assault was com-
mitted, Green was in a police-court several
miles away answering to another charge, and
therefore it was absolutely certain that he
couldn’t be the person wanted. As the court
Green was attending at the hour of the assault
was the same one where he was afterwards
charged with the said assault, it is impossible
to suppose that the police and the law autho-
rities, and the whole legal machine, or some of
it, were not aware of the position. Therefore,
one or two questions naturally arise : (1) By
whose instructions did the police apply to have
remanded, week after week, a man whom they
must (or ought to) have known to be innocent ?
(2) Was the real criminal busy getting away
during this long and unaccountable delay ?
And (3), seeing that the assaulted woman
and her son swore to Green, despite
his conclusive alibi and the fact that their
previous description didn’t fit him, as the
man who committed the assault, are any further
steps to be taken by the law ? There are a
whole lot of shabby little mysteries hanging on
to the skirts of the Dean case, which all help
to bring Botany Bay law into still more con-
tempt and public mockery.
The Dishonest “Hansard.”
Someone sends along the very excellent sugges-
tion that Hansard should be made a verbatim
instead of a Bowdlerised report of Parlia-
mentary proceedings. The papers (except when
it suits them) do not report Parliamentary
speeches with any semblance of fulness or
accuracy. Hansard purports to give all
speeches, but it gives them in a revised and
improved state, and many politicians whose
utterances are unadulterated drivel are saved
from the destruction they deserve simply
because the official records supply a refined
and glorified version of them, and endow them
with an intelligence that is not their own. If
Hansard told the plain, unadulterated truth—
if it gave in full the bad grammar, the lost
pronouns, the tangled sentences which never
came to any definite end, the “hems,” and
“ haws,” and “ ers,” the relatives which knew
not their own antecedents, the stutterings,
and dodderings, and mumblings, and feeble
repetitions, and hazy parentheses, and errone-
ous quotations, the errors in facts and statistics,
the groans, the bad language, the drivel and
washiness, and everything else that goes to
make up a true record it would not
only be much more interesting, but it
would help to stop much useless eloquence
and to abolish a lot of useless politicians.
There are a good many lengthy speakers who
would give up speaking or else speak a great
deal less if they were reported with merciless
accuracy, and there are a few whom an abso-
lutely accurate system of reporting would shut
out of the Legislature altogether. And, in any
case, Hansard ought to be an accurate record
in the interests of truth alone. The people pay
for the legislators and they should have a chance
to know what kind of legislators they get.
They also pay for the reports, and they should
get the pure article ; not an elaborate adultera-
tion.
P.M.G. Duffy, of Victoria, is raising an honest
penny for the revenue by allowing whisky and
tobacco advts. to be placed on the front of post-
cards. Therefore the Band of Hope and the
Rechabites and the other Cold Tea individuals
announce that their “ principles ” won’t allow
them to use post-cards, and the 1.0.G.T. and the
Y. VV.O.A. and the W.C.T.U. and the rest of
the alphabet are harrying Duffy with deputa-
tions on the subject. Yet these cheap, self-
advertising shams are allowed by their prin-
ciples to use tramcars that are plastered over
with beer and tobacco advts., and to use rail-
ways which have whisky puffs on the walls of
every station, and to read the daily and weekly
papers with drink notifications in almost every
column, and to walk through the streets where
drink advts. are on every hoarding, and to go to
theatres where they find them on the pro-
grammes, and to do many other like wicked-
nesses. So long as the W.C.T.U. can do all
these things without injury to its moral health,
then its statement that it can’t use post-cards
with liquor advts. on them is bumptious
hypocrisy. Moreover, all the liquor advts.
on earth won’t increase the aggregate sale of
the Accursed Thing; the public simply drinks as
much as it wants to or can afford to, anil the only
effect is that it may give up drinking the liquor
that isn’t advertised and take instead to the
one that is. The same rule applies in every
case where the article advertised is already in
universal use ; the object is not to increase the
aggregate trade, but to grab some other man’s
share of the trade already existing. Therefore,
the drink-sellers, by riotous advertising, are
simply struggling to cut each other’s throats,
and any W.C.T.U. with sense enough to come
in out of the wet would be pleased to see them
doing it. Probably, however, the real object
of that chaste, but angular, institution is to
convince itself and the public that it is still
alive ; in other words, it shakes its rusty
bonnet and its bulbous gamp at P.M.G. Duffy
for purely advertising purposes. The children
of light and Cold Tea have a keener eye for
cheap puffs than all the Much-Travelled
Smytiie family put together.
Dear Bulletin,— Melb. Age, a red-hot advo-
cate of the first Vic. State Bank scheme that
offers, says cheerfully : “ The form of manage-
ment suggested is a governor and deputy-
governor, wholly unconnected with other
financial institutions ; and a supervising board
consisting of the Chief Justice, the Speaker of
the Leg. Assembly, the Treasurer, and the
Attorney-General.” The Argus dismally re-
marks, “We have no wish to hurt feelings or
cause pain, but it is necessary, in the public
interest, to ask what a board of Speakers and
Treasurers would have given us in the past.
We should have had a State bank run by Mr.
Munro, Matthew Davies, and Mr. Bent.”
Also, G. D. Carter, Bryan O’Loghlen, and
Justice Madden, the wise acting-Gov. who
signed Patterson and Carter’s order for a com-
pulsory bank holiday, to allay the ’93 panic.
You, dear Bulletin, seem disposed to over-
look the fact that a badly-managed State bank
would prove the worst thing that ever hap-
pened to Victoria. And it would be shockingly
mismanaged. The policies of four successive
Governments in dealing with one or other kind
of financial or commercial dishonesty have
left the country no hope of a respectable
State bank. Public men are what public
opinion makes them, and present public
opinion in Victoria has been corrupted by a
combination of dirty circumstances. It is vain
to argue that public interests can be safeguarded
by rules and regulations, and auditors. Men
run banks, not rules or auditors. If the men
are no good, the rules are practically worthless
and the audit amounts to nothing at all. The
existing lot of Victorian inhabitants will have
to die and be replaced by creatures of a higher
moral tone all round (financially speaking)
before a State Bank scheme will commend itself
to any intelligent resident who hasn’t selfish
designs upon the capital.—B. Liste.”
The saddest thing about every big movement
is its ultimate success. The original Freetrade
agitation in England maintained a big office
and a staff of well-paid secretaries, clerks, lec-
turers, and writers. There was bitter conster-
nation in that office when the programme of
the party became law. No one wants to run
after an omnibus any more when he has caught
it, so the subscriptions stopped, and the clerks,
lecturers, secretaries, and pamphleteers were
“chucked out” miserably into a cold, cold
world. Similarly, when genuine Prohibition
arrives, there will be an end to the billets
of the teetotal-lecturers and secretaries, the
editors of the Cold Tea rags, the politicians
who get in on the teetotal vote, the W.C.T.U.
magnates, and all the rest; they will simply
drop into utter insignificance, because the one
subject they know anything about is finished.
If Freetrade ever becomes the absolutely settled
policy of New South Wales it will kill many
people, because they live solely by running
after that particular omnibus, and when they
have definitely caught it no one will pay them
to run after it any more. If the heathen really
become converted, then the missionary busi-
ness, with its salaries and its perks, is dead.
When woman gets the suffrage, then no one
can become famous by being a woman-suffragist
any more. When the Single-tax comes then
Henry George may as well suicide. For the
person of one fad success is a truly awful thing.
Gov. Brassey must be densely ignorant of
financial facts, if he isn’t poking borak at his
Smellbourne admirers. The other night he
stood up, without a smile on his face, or a
chuckle in his voice, and told the Chamber of
Commerce that the land-boom was a blessing in
disguise, because “it had given the people of
Victoria a chance, nobly used, of proving to
the world that they are resolved, at all sacri-
fices, to meet all engagements—(cheers) —and
maintain their credit.” Flesh and blood
cannot stand by and permit these awful perver-
sions of the truth to be performed by the best
Governor on earth. The Bulletin is not in-
disposed to approve of Gov. Brassey in moder-
ation, as long as he doesn’t play into the hands
of the—er—the wrong ’uns, so to speak.
Whilst the nautical new-chum seven-or-nine-.
times-millionaire confines himself to whole-
sale truth and harmless nonsense, this
paper will never smite him unawares with
a brick. But if, after making due enquiries
concerning the prosperous whitewashed bank-
rupts and bloated private compositioners who
swarmed into his recent levee, through the
main entrance or private door—if, after learn-
ing how many finks in the £ those loyal sub-
jects paid, or didn’t pay when called upon to
meet their engagements, Brassey should still
class the malodorous supporters of vice-royalty
with the decent people of Victoria, then The
Bulletin will rain fire and brimstone upon the
bebaubled time-server.
Old Hori Grey, who, all his life, has loathed
pomp and show and advt., is to have a statue
erected to him at Auckland. The Women’s
Liberal League started the idea, and out of the
.£lOOO required for a cheap and very ordinary
image, it has with great difficulty got together
£lOO. If that statue is put up Grey will never
return to Auckland to see it if he can con-
veniently stay away, and if, for want of funds,
it never goes up at all, the poor old Tribune’s
name will have been dragged around by
the poke-bonnetted brigade to no earthly
purpose. The statue business is a stupid
craze at the best. The average bronze
figger is hideously inartistic and looks
a great deal less picturesque than a gum-tree,
and it costs a lot, and is of no use to anybody.
When the public wants to honor a great man
it should raise the money to buy up a dingy
slum, and convert it into a wide street with
trees on each side and then name it after the
hero of the hour. The statue idea is preferred
princinally because the local Mayor Smith
wants" to do the unveiling ceremony and to
have his ignoble cognomen mentioned on the
pedestal.
As the Australian rabbit can climb trees,
will some expert explain what can be the earthly
use of a wire-netting fence entirely unprotected
and uncrowned by a spiked top-piece?
Ed. Bulletin, —Be the Northern limit of
the platypus. Some three years ago one was
caught alive in the Burdekin, just above Charters
Towers, and 12 months ago one (a youngster) was
shot on the Fletcher, 25 miles north of that town.
This brings the limit up to the 10th parallel. Old
aborigines say they have seen them still further
north in the reedy marshes of the Little Fletcher.
—N.Q.

The dauntless Eisenstadter, whose name was
once a household word in every Australian mining region
as a goldfields pioneer, is dead at last. In his day he
erected more stamper batteries than any other man. He
was the first to put up a mill (in the early Fifties) on
Forest. Creek. In ’6l lie erect* d mills at Woods Point—
the greater part of Ihe machinery being packed on horse-
back in sections, and the heavier portions conveyed in
sledces over savage anil roadless ranges. From the
Morning Star claim at the Point he drew in dividends
14,0000 z. gold. Pushing on, he set up mills at Raspberry
Creek m the same distiict. and at Alexandria ; in’67 he
was at Upp- r Bendoc, where lie er'Cted the Phoenix 10-
head. Or-nf II was his next seal of operations; then
Junee reefs, a;, noth of wh'oh places he erected extensive
works. Hid End and Tamharoora, where he invested
largely in machinery, next caught his attention; then
Fagan’s Creek and Little River (Braidwood district),
where he erect'd ha I t'ri' s, pumping and winding gear in
’Bl. At Quaripot Creek, near Slanthorpe iQ.). he had
three engines at woik on stream tin, and rea'is* d a large
amount from same; at Tulgin and Windiyer he also
erected large plains; also at Charters Tow. rs (’75), and
still later near Rockhampton. Many a min* r has cause
to remember grab fully the name of the gallant old
Eisenstadl' r.

A BEAUTIFUL COMPLEXION.
It has long been known to scientists that for
improving the appearance and beautifying the
face oatmeal is far and away superior to all other
foods, but it had one drawback*- the tendency to
heat tiie blood. To overcome this defect the at-
tention of eminent scientists has been devoted to
the removal of this imperfection ; and now, after
long years of careful and diligent research, art has
triumphed, and Uncle Toby’s Rolled Oats is the
result, for, by skilful manipulation in the manu-
facture. all tile heating properties have been ex-
tracted, so that now a beautiful complexion of
peach and ivory mav be had by anyone who m d.
a continuous use of Uncle Toby’s Rolled Oats.

One night when the Austral with Gov.
Hampden on board was wallowing heavily in the
Great Australian Bight the vice regal daughters
took alarm, ami came in a parti ally-dressed state
to their father’s cabin. There they hung on in
terror to the door-handle and called out wildly :
“ Gome out, pa ; pa, we’re sinking!” Whereupon
Gov. Hampden emerged, a stately and profoundly
calm figure in pyjamas, and called loudly for the
steward. That functionary presently turned up.
“Steward,”said the great man in a serious tone ;
“Isay, steward, are you quite sure this —ah—
vessel is—ah—propahly packed ?”

The Bicycle Boom,
Sydney is now fairly in the midst of the bicycle
boom that started in Adelaide with the advent of
Zimmerman, and is likely to keep the people of
the different colonies in a state ot excitement for
some months. These colonies have experienced
the result of several booms of late years, but with
disastrous results, as the folk from the Cabbage
Garden well know. The greatest of all booms
that Australia has seen is the rush for the B.L.
Corto Rothschild Cigars, which are a delightful
smoke and obtainable from all first-class tobac-
conists at fid. each. The sales of these cigars are
tremendous; during Cup week in Melbourne they
amounted to 1,000,000, and this is not to be
wondered at considering the quality of the smokes
and the tobacco employed being grown in the
finest Havanas district.*
SUNDRY SHOWS.

Exhibition Building Oriental Carnival.
Arthur Dacre, the well-known English
actor, suicided with a razor last Sunday, in a
Macquarie-street (Sydney) boarding-house, after
killing his wife with a revolver. The Dacres
(he was about 45, and she hardly 41) were
both fine artistes in their own particular line
of high-class comedy. Their terrible end was
surrounded by so many pathetic circumstances
and the pair were so much esteemed by all who
made their acquaintance that it can safely be said
that no occurrence of the sort which has taken
place in Australia within the memory of the pre-
sent generation has so widely and deeply stirred
public sympathy. In sordid business matters
they were luckless and incompetent, and the last
year or two of their lives was a long series of
financial misfortunes. Their last engagement was
with the “Land of the Moa ” co. at Sydney
Royal, and this descent into melodrama as a last
resource—when it became evident that there was
no present market for high-class comedy in Aus-
tralia — preyed on Dacre’s mind. He was
also harassed by previous disappointments,
by the illness of Ins wife for whom be
entertained an intense and chivalrous affec-
tion, and by fears for their joint future, and a
hopeless, half-crazed sense that their career was
practically closed. Few men were ever less able
to face serious trouble, and, in his last days, poor
Dacre was wont to pour out his griefs to almost
everybody he met. The sombre; tragic character
of Dean Maitland, which lie was studying for the
next Royal drama, did not serve as a tome to bis
shattered nerves, so on the Sunday he laid down
his long-drawn-out unfinished part and ended Ids
wife’s troubles and Ids own together. She was
found dead on the bed with a handkerchief over
her eyes, where she had perhaps placed it so that
she might not see what was coming; while her
husband, with his carotid artery severed, was just
about expiring. His last words were a pathetic
request to he lifted on to the bed and laid beside
her. So far as all the signs go, it was a case of
death by mutual consent.
• * *
Dacre’s medical training made the tragedy
sudden and painless. He shot his wife through
the heart, and he severed his own carotid artery in-
stead of wounding his throat in the clumsy manner
of most would-be suicides. The romantic affection
which subsisted between him and his wife is ex-
pressed in the epitaph lie desired should be inscribed
on their tombstone, “They loved each other,
and in their death they were not divided.” Which
one, among his accumulated troubles and disap-
pointments, it was that really killed Dacre no one
probably will ever know for certain, but Ids refined
and sensitive nature made him worry himself to
distraction over small obligations, and “Dean
Maitland ” attacking his over-wrought brain pro-
bably had something to do with the terrible finale.
** * *
The Lyceum management has wisely taken
down its weak-kneed American drama, and puts
up a comedy in its stead. The writer of “ One of
Our Girls” had something to say, and had fortu-
nately not got it so tightly jammed in his mind
that lie couldn’t get it out, and he says it brightly
and intell gently. The play is a bitter satire on
the French upper-class marriage system, as
■evidenced in the case of Miss Grace Noble, whose
aristocratic parents hand her over to a bad,
'blotched Count, whom she had only seen once
'before in her life, and, as the gas wasn't lighted
■on that occasion, she only saw his shadow against
'the dusk even then. They are married, and the
blotched Count looms wickedly around through
■three acts and scrowls heavilyon his pansy-blossom.
Miss Helen Danvray, as an American niece, per-
vades the play by way of contrast, and shows the
.American view of matrimony, and leads a bnll-in-
a-china-shop existence in the elevated French
'family, and serves as a haven of refuge for the
■crushed little Countess when her blotched noble-
itnan is a worse nightmare than usual. The latter
■gentleman’s career is cut short by the discovery
that he has dropped his first wife into his second
father-in-law’s supply of drinking-water, and when
the curtain falls he is understood to lie scowling
liri bed with a bullet in his chest and the eye of
the law upon him. He is described throughout
■as a person of very old family, and his doings
■suggest that the family is altogether too old and
ihas gone bad.
# • •
There are only three notable parts in “ One
■of Our Girls.” Miss Helen Dauvray, as the
American girl, has one of them, and her bright-
mess and dash make it the best half of the drama.
The scowling Count is another character who has
;a good deal of meat in him. King Hedley plays
Ihim impressively with a wicked smile and a quan-
tity of damp black hair, but lie speaks in a muffled,
underhand tone, and cultivates a motionless frame
■of mind which is overdone at times. Alias Grace
Noble is a pretty piece of fluffiness and emotion as
the French wife, and the rest are mostly a maze
■of little characters. Flton is wasted as a
small, cynical doctor ; Granville Blake and
Scott Inalis are two good young men, who
are not allowed to say anything very amus-
ing ; C. Berkeley is a new and elaborate idiot, and
Miss Nelly Mortyne does some good w<>rk as a
shocked French parent who is always falling back
against something. The count s first wife doesn t
appear ; all that is heard of her is a shriek in the
back premises as she falls into the well. Ihe
shrieks that come from the back premises, if they
are properly applied, help out the realism con-
siderably, anil few people consider that the cause
of them is generally a super who is sitting on a
packing-case among the wings, possibly with a
pot of refreshment in his hand, and screaming
wildly for help in between times.
