Sydney fashionables are till agog over the
production by locil society amateurs of a new
comic operetta, entitled “Club Life,’’ music by
Ernest Truman (a Sydney “boy”), and libretto
by “ The Banjo.” The tenor and soprano parts
are taken by professionals, but all the rest of the
singers (including a chorus of 15 beautiful society
damsels), are amateurs. The dresses are very
stri <ing, and if the amateurs don’t make too much
of a hash of it this new departure for Australian
talent should be a success.
** » »
“The Gondoliers,” with a new tenor,
trained in the Gilbertian school of comic-opera, to
play a name-part, was Saturday’s hill at Melbourne
Princess’s. Courtice Pounds, the stranger in
question, is a plump and pleasing songster, and a
comedian of much more than the average tenor’s
ability. He has a large, round head, a serio-comic
smile, and his speaking method, although effemiu-
ately gushing, adapts itself to humorous expres-
sion. Also, lie dances with a light fantastic toe,
like all plump men who have learned to dance at
all. Ouurtioe Pounds, in short, is somewhat of a
rarity, a tenor-buffo if necessary, who .could act
the barber in “Madame Angot ” and do justice
to the charming air (usually omitted) which ought
to be incidental to that character. He doesn't
quite equal Charles “Take a pair of
sparkling eyes,” but be is the best of several
Marcos who have tried to interpret that
descriptive ditty on the opera stage hereabouts.
The new tenor’s first appearance was a promising
success. Miss Graupner and Miss Ida Osborne
popped up again in tbeir original places as the
gondoliers’ wives. 'Jibe smaller of these two little
ladies has lost a top note in the meantime, nor
does Miss Osborne give out the musical text as
sonorously as she did, but, in other matters of
detail, they are wiser and happier than formerly.
Yet, how small they are ! Miss Candida Florence
Young towers above them when all ‘three stand
together in a row. And Florence would have had
to tilt her chin to ta’k to several of the dear
C- ee now departed. This is drifting into
irrelevance, however. Wallace Brownlow doesn’t
glitter as the baritone gondolier, but the other
principals are old fiiettds on their best behavior,
and the opera has not relaxed its grip of public
favor. Indeed, some of the author’s jokes are
now being jfenerally understood for the first time.

possible date.
«- * * «
“ Youth ” seems likely to run into a third
week at the Royal. For practical business pur-
poses, “Youth” returns as a novelty to Melbourne
after an 11 years’ absence. Besides, it is a sane and
intelligent production with bits of character, and
touches of eternal truth, and a really comic Irish-
man in it. The elderly clergyman, who claims
credit for having once upon a time cheerfully
abandoned his lady friend at duty’s call, and
therefore begs his son to repent and do likewise,
is a fine type of Christianity. Also, the trouble
that overtakes George Rignold when he
marries an improper person, instead of leaving
her in the lurch, serves to remind all similar
offenders that their sin will surely find them
out. “Youth” is a rather neatly - written
lesson in worldly prudence and the necessity of
looking after Number One. The two acts devoted
to military glory have no apparent connection
with the drama’s moral principles, but they get
there all the same. The embarking of the troops,
and the subsequent destruction of sundry untu-
tored t-avages by Rignold and his brave com-
panions, are incidents not easily forgotten, on
account of the brass music and prevailing tumult.
“ Youth,” l>y the wav, lias been brought right up
to date. A young lady of doubtful character is
asked why she didn't come to a social gathering
on her hike, and she says that she left the bike at
home, because the other girls get so dreadfully
jealous when she sports her “ ratiouals.

If Amy Roselle Daore wasn’t a Jewess, she much belied
her looks. She was unhappily not seen at anything like
he best in Australia. Her best days had probably pa-sed,
but in England a few years ago she occupied a very high
position in the drama—was good enough, for instance, to
appear with the greatest acceptance in foremost parts on
the stage of the very theatre at which Ellen Terry was
queen. Her husband, Arthur Daore (real name, Arthur
Culver James), was a legally-qualified physician. When
complaining to a Sydney friend some weeks ago of his
misfortunes in Australia, lie was asked whether lie had
ever thought of resuming practice as a doctor. “No,”
he said, rather aadly, “1 have forgotten tno much, and
there is so much I have neglected to learn. Besides, I
am afraid I have lost my nerve.”

X.Z. : “ Hypnotist Kennedy has at least three excellent
acrobats in Ins troupe of subjects. One, the game-legged
young man who does the catalepsy business, is especially
good, but tbal act has two faults. In a true cataleptic,
the limbs and body remain rigid in the positions liny
were in at the outset, and, though a leg or arm can be
s lifted to a different angle, it moves as a rigid whole; but
ju -t before Kennedy sits astride the alleg. d cataleptic, lie
bends the body and legs into the best po-dtions for carry-
ing weight. With a true cataleptic that could not be
done. Secondly, if you placed a true cataleptic so as to
r st with the line hack of his head on one chair and the
heels of bis boots on another, he would remain quite rigid
and support weights well; but Kennedy’s subject does not
rest so. His rest is with that mass of powerful muscle
between the mot of the neck and the shoulder-blades on
the one cbair-bai k, anil that very strong spot just above
the heels (tendon of Achilles) on the other. He thus gets
a splendid acrobatic hold with the neck and shoulders at
one end and w ith the hook f >rmed by the shinbones and
heel at the other. So much for show mesmerism.”

It is not absolutely impossible that the
Dean alleged-conspiracy case may be heard before
Mr. Justice Innes, who, so far, has (fortunately
for himself) not been mixed up with the matter at
a ‘l-. .His Honor has been for some months re-
cruiting at Bathurst, and is now, people will be
glad to hear, much improved in health. If, how-
ever, Judge Innes is at all superstitious he will
carefully keep out of the case. The Chief Justice
can’t very well sit, having been consulted re the
Dean-Meagher confession; of course Judge Win-
deyer cannot sit, and Judge Simpson happens to
be closely related to Salomons, Q.C. It would
also, for obvious reasons, be inadvisable for
Acting-Justice Cohen to take a hand in the affair.
Judge Manning, unless prevented by his ex-
tremely bad health, may take the case, which will
probably come on within 10 days. But it would
be very mun’j better for the province to borrow a
Judge for Che occasion.

Melb. Y.M.C.A.’s Brassey-grovel landed
that pious organisation in a loss of .£65 through
the non-attendance of the Governor and the con-
sequent absence of the large, bloated people who
were to pay 21s. each for the privilege of sitting

altogether, “ until God inspires someone to con-

inspired by God to continue a “ work ” which
consists so largely of grovelling to Brassey is cer-
tainly a humorous blasphemer.

* • #
The solemn humor of the S. A. clergy was
recently exemplified in their decision to set apart
Nov. 10 for prayers for rain—a time when a
general downpour would have ruined the hay-
crops then being reaped. S.A. farmers indig-
nantly disclaimed any business connection with
the parsons on this account, and a general chorus
of condemnation also came from the northern
wheat districts, owing to the fact chat it was too
late for rain to save the wheat-crops, but just in
time for it to revive the locust plague and destroy
what little wheat there was to garner.
• # #
New Botany Bay maxim : The curse of
“justice ” never did run smooth.
Formerly the dude of the city
was the bank-clerk ; now, the afore-
said bank-clerk is mostly a mealy-
mouthed, callow and teetotal youth,
with a soulful yearning for prayer-
meetings patronised by his general-
manager. The gorgeous bank-clerk
of the boom-time has been carefully
weeded out of the various staffs,
or has found such a strict super-
vision kept over the stamps and
commission accounts that life has
become irksome and tailors un-
usually aggressive.

The Protestant nuns become
more numerous. “ Sister Hannah ”
was dedicated to the order of
Methodist Sisterhood with porten-
tous solemnity in Melbourne the
other night, the Rev. Fitchett boss-
ing the ordination. “Sister Han-
nah ” is now supposed to be wedded
to the church from which there is
no divorce, and has been warned
by the Wesleyan Archbishop to
“ remember the fate of the woman
who looked back,” a hint to Hannah
that she must never hope for a
more material and tangible spouse
for fear of being turned into a
packet of salts, or something of the
kind.
* * *
Police-court lawyers are said
to be sometimes conscience-stricken.
One, now dead, whose practice lay
exclusively at the old Sydney Cen-
tral, received £lo—a princely sum
for him—to defend a well-connected
rascal, charged with assaulting a
little girl. When the child was in
the witness-box he examined her
closely, and, when finished, an-
nounced to the Bench that he
believed every word the child said,
and threw up his “ brief ” for the
defence. But he did not throw up
the fee.

* * *
Ballarat did its suffering on account of
Albert Edward’s birthday on Friday instead of
Saturday, the real day appointed by Providence.
The Friday half-holiday is the settled policy of
Ballarat, established with a thoroughness prob-
ably equalled nowhere else. Only fruit shops,
tobacconists and hotels open—just as if it were
Sunday. The local population regard Friday as
an impossible day to shop on, though the shops
open in the morning for the sake of the moths;
and the practice attempted for a time of opening
on Friday afternoon, when another holiday
occurred in the week, was quickly abandoned.
The plan adopted is to celebrate all holidays, in-
cluding Whit Monday, on Friday. Strangers find
it rather extraordinary to see New Year’s Day
celebrated in December, and Xmas and Boxing
Day separated by a week, but the Ballarat folk
must have their own way. It is now proposed
to abolish Sunday as a holiday in favor of Friday,
and there is much to be said for the change.
Ballarat weather is always too bad on Sunday to
go to church (the weather always goes to church
in other places) ,and it is always fine enough on
Friday for the perilous passage on a horse-tram
to the Wendouree rushes.

Last year, President R. 0. Baker, of S.A.
Leg. Council, declined to ask Premier Kingston
to his annual dinner, and, as a consequence, the
Speaker and Chairman of Committees of the
Assembly, with some other persons, stopped
away. This year, Baker gets over the difficulty
by confining his invitations to the Council, the
vice-regal party, and the press.

Now that the Melb. police defray travel-
ling expenses out of their own pockets, victims of
street accidents or sudden indisposition, if they
have a leg to stand on, are escorted to the nearest
hospital on foot, instead of by tram or cab as
formerly. On the day of Gov. Brassey’s landing,
an urchin who got mixed up with the St. Kilda
traffic and sustained painful scalp injuries, was
marched off by a member of the force to the
Homoeopathic Hospital, howling dismally and
apparently hard put to it to keep up with his long-
legged companion.

The hanging of a woman during “ Carnival
week ” was so obviously inappropriate to the
occasion that the Melbourne dailies said as little
as possible about it. Mrs. Williams met her doom
on the Monday, and a full, gruesome report in the
Cup morning issue was not to be thought of. It
might have turned a few people’s stomachs against
the races.

IN THE VINEYARD.
The Bulletin here prints an authentic photograph, recently
taken in Samoa by a Sydney amateur, of a Church of England
colored missionary. The picture will enable the pious ladies who
supply funds for the conversion of the heathen to perceive that all
their money is not spent in waistcoats. His reverence, it will he
observed, is a real parson, and “ has got ’em all on ” —holy hat,
sacred gamp, orthodox coat, and he carries under his arm seven
or eight pounds of the Word. Also, he is an unsophisticated
shepherd, and evidently possesses most rudimentary ideas as to the
proper manner of wearing his white necktie.
When Gov. Brassey first arrived in Melb.,
he talked at large about the desire of himself and
Lady B. to do good to everybody. But in the
latest Brassey utterance we have it that the Gov.’s
wife “has come to Victoria anxious to do all she
can in the sphere in which she moves for the good
of Victoria.”

THE LAST TO CONFESS.
I must be in fashion ; confession’s the thing—
Like tan-shoes and johnnies, divorce and the ring ;
Hello ! Are you ready?—then riddle me this—
-1 came out of nothing and go back to bliss :
For I’m a Mahatma—please whisper it low—
And lived my last life where Mahatmas do grow,
And so what the jury are waiting to find,
1 see written clearly across the man’s mind.
My quaint astral body I send into space,
And hear its conclusions ere hearing the case.
The long hours of Court-work seem like unto years.
And oft-times my yawnings are taken for tears.
I often have wondered why nobody guessed ;
And now—treat me kindly, because I’ve confessed!
Then the telephone rang-off abruptly, and ever
since The Bulletin has been wondering who
could possibly have been at the other end of the
wire.
THE KIND SKIPPER.
A jovial song of a Guv’nor brave,
The sorcy Sunbeam’s skipper !
’Tis said as ’ow, on the boundin’ wave,
He’ll shake his fust mate’s flipper.
And larf aloud when the waves do roar
To see ’ira thus unbendin’ ;
As a tar afloat, or a lord ashore,
He’s right-down condescendin’.
At Melbourne port, the other day,
This briny toff was landed,
And all the people cried “ ’Ooray !”
As good Queen Vic. commanded ;
They begged of ’im to boss the show,
Which ’e, their cause befriendin’,
Agreed to do, come weal or woe—
He’s right-down condescendin’.
He takes the ’elrn, quite simple-like,
No soshul dooties shirkin’,
And now and then he rides a bike.
To keep his liver workin’.
And if he gives the nobs a ball
They sees their skipper wendin’
His way around, with smiles for all—
He’s right-down condescendin’.
So true ’is ’eart to ocean foam.
Last week he "got invited
To patronise the Seamen’s Ome,
And there ’is lordship sighted
A sailor from the Sunbeam dear,
Whose presence warn’t offendin’;
The chairman smiled, and said “ Wot cheer !”
He’s right-down condescendin’.
No Saxon churl this Guv’nor brave—
His blood is Norman-racey,
He took the name ’is father gave
And turned it into “ Bracey.”
But though no lord wot ploughs the main
Has got more brass for spendin’,
We’d ’ave you understand again
He’s right-down condescendin’.

* * *
Some of the small Victorian sects have
been holding a day of humiliation and prayer for
rain. The large, massive churches are not in the
movement, which is confined almost entirely to
Bethsaida Chapel, and Bethel, and Bethany meet-
ing-house, and the Little Ehenezer, and the like.
Also, the prayer has somehow been postponed
until the crops are practically lost, and there is
little for the rain to rain upon save the bare
ground and the skeleton sheep and the overdraft.
There is a great want of generalship about the
business. It should have been started two months
earlier, and the entire praying horse-power of all
the sects should have been turned on in one terrific
blast, with a long, strong pull and a pull all
together.
* # #
There was a Baptist Union Conference in
Melb. last week, whereat a certain Rev. J. C.
Martin got upon the prevailing drought subject,
and gave the country some idea as to what the
Almighty means to do under the distressing cir-
cumstances. Rev. Martin, although viewing the
drought with regret, has not the least doubt that
“IT ALL DEPENDS.”
Gov. H AMPDEN (who rides) asks: “ Can I yet safe mounts for my daughters in Australia?”
“God will give the people strength to bear it.
Which is both reassuring and reasonable. The
Divine Providence that enables human nature to
put up with drivelling shepherds may be expected
to give agriculturists strength to survive the
drought. After all, it is mainly a question of
money or credit. The Lord is the storekeeper or
the bank. A small fanner would die of the
drought if his tucker supply were cut off, and a
squatter gets very shaky when the overdraft
stops payment.
# • *
The awful gathering of Smellbourne’s
anti-drinkers, anti-smokers, and anti-everything-
elsers in protest against post-card advertisements
is very significant. These are the people who
religiously refrain from sending twopenny letters
when penny cards will do as well. It is a case of
the Revenue being asked to forego a chance of
making a little money out of consideration for the
cant of its natural enemies, the Mean Brigade-
in a word, the temperance saints. Let ’em buy the
advt. space on the post-cards for themselves, and
substitute “ Eternity ” for the names of beers and
tobaccos. Ordinary sinners wouldn’t object.
* * *
Afc the recent funeral of a very prominent
brother, the local Orangemen down South, not
being able to get an imposing muster of the lodges
at short notice, hit upon the happy plan of hiring
a host of theatrical supers and rigging them up
in the yellow-jaundice regalia. The antics of the
brethren and pseudo-brethren around the grave
were described by onlookers as ridiculous and dis-
graceful. ♦
• • •
When Gov. Gormanston (who weighs some-
thing over 20st.) quits Tasmania, he will leave a
considerable-sized piece of flesh behind him in the
land of his temporary adoption. That ample
viceroy went Ashing on the Derwent recently, and
in a moment of excitement he sat down on his new
patent fish-hook just imported from England.
The implement of torture had to be cut out with a
pen-knife, and a pretty large piece of the Queen s
representative was cut out along with it and
thrown overboard. There is no dignity about the
vice-regal office under such conditions, and a
Governor with a fish-hook in him yells just like
any private citizen.
• * *
Last Saturday two suspended Sydney
solicitors applied to be re-admitted to practice,
and though the applications were decisively re-
jected by the Full Court the fact that they were
made stands as a record of the unspeakable gall
of the “ Gent One.” Both the solicitors got into
trouble through annexing their clients’ money—
the client in one case being a widow, and the
money annexed (£200) being every penny .she
possessed. The solicitors weren’t gaoled for their
mean thefts ; they were simply suspended in a
temporary way with an understanding that their
cases would be considered when they had paid
back the stealings. Neither solicitor paid back the
stealings, however, but both asked to be reinstated
on the ground of poverty, and the principal fraud
of the pair pleaded that unless he was allowed to
practise (and have a chance of getting at the money
of other clients without paying back the cash he
had already taken), “ it did not appear likely that
he would ever again be able to hold up his head as
an honest man.” This sounds pathetic enough,
and yet, if he had been anything else save a
solicitor, he would probably have been doing five
years’ gaol, and no one would have dreamt of
troubling about his head, except to see that it was
shaved regularly.

Misses HSlfreda Hilda Gamble, Janet
Lindsay Greig. and Ida Gertrude Margaret
Halley are the three latest women-graduates in
medicine of Melb. University. Miss Gamble is a
daughter of a Vic. school-inspector, and Miss
Halley of a well-known Wesleyan minister. (Miss
Greig’s younger sister will also graduate jn rueni-
cine in about a month’s time.) This brings up
the list of Vic. women-doctors to 13, of whom all
but one are Melb. graduates. That one is the
wife of the Rev, D. Egryn Jones, of Melb who,
as Emma Constance Stone, studied with, gra-
duated in medicine with, and married her hus-
band at Toronto.

Ed, Bulletin,— As to your remark re
“Gen.” Booth’s comfortable and luxurious sur-
roundings and his female officers’ poverty and
privations in bush towns. In my backblocks
travels, I’ve met with most pitiful oases of dis-
tress amongst Salvation (female) officers. Weak-
minded but often good, honest women, living in
draughty humpies, on a meal a day or less, and
clad in merely sufficient rags to satisfy common
decency. In a Bogan River town I’ve known a
female “captain ” and “lieutenant ” live for three
days on one loaf ot bread and some fat. The only
female Salvationist who can enjoy more than a
“cadged” existence is the pretty one who can
trade upon her looks. To her the Mulgoa folks
throw their money. But good looks being a rarity
amongst Salvo’s, the majority of the women live a
wretched life—at times almost on the verge of
starvation. —R.H., Bourke.

uncle—an old and respected resident, and a 1-ay

with conspiracy.

The interesting Paterson divorce - suit
came to a sudden end last week owing to the pro-
duction of a letter written by Mrs. Paterson to a
Sydney private-detective, in which she suggested
that he might be able, for a reasonable cash pay-
ment “to get a woman to lead Paterson
on.” The detective handed over the letter
and the case straightway broke down. ihe
business of hiring someone to lead another
person on is evidently at a discount smce
the disastrous collapse of the Butler trial, ihe
Paterson case, so far as the published evidence
goes, seems to be the story of a jealous, bad-
tempered woman who spent her life mostly in
looking round a, corner in hopes of finding her
husband winking at the servant, and the end ot it
was interesting chiefly because it afforded two
barristers an opportunity of showing how deeply
shocked they were at the wickedness of a client
who wanted to have her husband led on. And this,
though the law every day of the week allows
policemen in plain clothes and their fizgigs to lead
on publicans to illegal acts, and, when those acts
are accomplished, takes full advantage of its
position.
Mrs. Paterson, by the way, has been struck by
the Dean curse. Private-detective Cook’s action
in having the Chief Justice consulted as to whether
it was his duty to hand over to the Court the
compromising letter written him by the lady
would appear to have been inspired by the
action of Salomons, Q. 0., when he
the Chief Justice as to attorney Meagher s
confession. Cook is the detective entitled
to the credit of tracking down and land-
ing in gaol Butler and his fellow conspirators.
Mrs. Paterson, formerly Gill, was before her mar-
riage, in ’BB, a teacher of cookery at Sydney
Technical College, earning .£4OO a year ; respon-
dent, Hugh Paterson, is a fashionable Sydney
dentist, who admitted an annual income of about
,£lBOO. In ’94, a deed of separation was execiited,
under which the lady got an allowance of £.400
a year, and subsequently £3OO. It was partly,
Booth’s Army consists of a fatly-fed staff, ever
jaunting and junketing, and a mass of mendicant
field officers, able-bodied men and women, who
hardly ever get any wages, and live on charity.

A great grievance among Northern miners is
the excessive fees charged at every turn by Govt. Apropos
of which Browne, M.L. A. (fog'.): “ Up North, in the office
of a certain warden was a clock. It was a beautiful clock
there were reliable rumors that the sun rose and set by
it. The miners used to regulate their watches by it. But
the fee business killed it dead. It still keeps good time,
but the miners consult it no more. Why? Because of
the fees! If seen consulting it they’re afraid they’d be
billed.”

One J. Brown signs his name to a remarkable
statement published in a Q. paper. He says he took up
a goldfields’ homestead holding at Cloncurry 19 years
ago, has lived there ever since and always paid up. Now
he has been turned out because a squatter, with thou-
sands of acres, wanted it for pasture puipases. Having
to re-build, he asked said squatter for the use of the
yards in the meantime, but was refused. He got six
ni nths’ notice to quit, he says, but what was the use of
that when he hadn’t got the valuation to go on with?
Also, the Govt, actually appointed the squatter valuator
to value the property he was going to pay for 1

The town of Gympie had extended so much
that it became necessary to close the cemetery (located
in the centre of gold-bearing reefs) and start another in a
different locality ; whereupon a number of Gympieites
had their friends’ remains removed to the new God’s
acre. One evening a miner was returning home after
removing the remains of his brother Joe, from which he
had taken a small bone and put same in his pocket.
Meeting a well-known veteran mine-manager he handed
him the bone and asked his opinion of it. The manager
took it, viewed it all over, remarked that it must be some
kind of soft brown slate ; and then proceeded to break it
with his teeth. “Hold on,” exclaimed the miner,
“ that s Joe ! ” “What’s Joe?” enquired the manager.
"My brother, Joe,” retorted the digger, “that was
buried in the old cemetery about 10 years ago. I’ve been
removing the poor fellow to the new C‘ metery, and that
is a small bone from some part of his body.” Moral;
The wisest expert can be puzzled by an odd specimen,
seen out of its site and surroundings.

nigher figure in the lottery-prospectus, W. R. Wilson

up by members of the syndicate that won the pro-
perty in the “distribution,” It VV.R. had been disposed
to drive a hard bargain he might, no doubt, have got the
place back again for a good deal less money than he paid.
£24,000 was his ultimatum. When the syndicate found
that he had finished bidding (through an agent) it
withdrew its last forcing offer of £25 000, and Wilson’s
paradise was regained. Unlucky nurchasers of St. Alban’s
lottery-tickets can console themselves with the reflection
that they stood a chance of capturing a £24,000 prize.

At Cape Otway (Vic.) a prospecting-party, out
for the last two years, has found gold in the debris when
driving in the hope of picking up a reef. Such persistency
deserves to be rewarded with a big find. A good reefing
field in the Otway forest would mean the opening up of
an extensive area of agricultural country, now pretty
well given over to the timber-splitters.
• • • »
Bear Bulletin, —Re a recent Bulletin para-
graph : Castlemaine reefs have never had a fair chance.
Hardly a shaft in the place has gone down past 400 ft. ;
and many cases could be cited of mines in other places
which did not pay between the surface and deep levels.
Look at the Star of the East in Ballarat, and the bad
time it passed through before it got 1000 to 2000 oz. at a
hig depth. The deepest mine in Castlemaine district is
the Ajax (1300 ft.), in the hands of Lanseil, of Bendigo.
And Lanseil, whose No. 180 at B»ndigo was the first Vic.
mine to get half-a-mile down, ought to be a reliable man
in deep-sinking. Bendigo mines are payable as far down
asSOOOft. Castlemaine has not paid between 300 ft. and
500 ft., and so everybody “funks” trying further. But
things are looking less dead just now. The Old Quartz
Hii), which used to go 20oz. to the ton, has been found to
give |oz. to the ton from its quartz refuse; and the
South Wattle Gully, which more than paid working ex-
penses four years ago, and has supported tributors since,
is being syndicated afresh. It will be time enough to call
ns “ only surface ” when we have had a show at bigger
d-pths.—W.
In ’94 Vic. employed 27,856 miners— best on
record since 'B4. Ballarat, top, 6317; Bendigo, fourth,
4282—both Castlemaine and Maryborough leading the
latter by a few hundreds. Chinese are deserting mining,
their number in ten years having fallen from 5359 to 2228.
Expected this year’s record will show a big increase in
the mining population.

Lanseil, of Bendigo, Is a director in 35 local

ot labor covenants. Lansell says he has so far dropped

• * • »
Lansell’s famous 180 is now down 3200 odd feet.
In the fifties Theo. Rallerstedt and son picked up the 180
for £3O, sank lower, woo an immense fortune, and, think-
ing the mine played out, sold it to Lansell for £30,000.
First slope he worked yielded £lBO,OOO profit I But then
how much of that has his show mine, the 222, swallowed
up? Lansell one day to the writer: ‘‘l took £90,000 out
of that hole (180) and put it into that hole (222) over
there.”
Lansell and his two brothers originally started
in Bendigo as butchers, with a soap-factory as a side-
show. Lucky specs, in the Advance and Cinderella mines
added the first big pile to George’s fortune, and he dthy
dies, from the rich Garden Gully United (paid £696,315 in
divs.) topped it off. When the Garden was in the
“dumps," he held a heavy share swag, and to lighten the
load actually paid a friend £2 10s. to accept 5000 and the
liability on them. That parcel represented a fortune,
and a few years later the acceptor returned to England to
invest his wealth in a palatial residence and estate.

S. A. Justice Bundey has ruled that, in distribut-
ing the assets of a voluntarily wound-up mining co., paid-
up shareholders rank first, i.e., their initial whack con-
sisting of the excess paid up on their shares over the con-
tribs. Balance to be apportioned equally among share-
holders on the basis of their representation. This decision
was given in the matter of the Royal Mint and Iron King
Co., which had £30,000 and 37,000 shares in the English
co., which bought the property to whack out.

A committee representing the Melbourne
and suburban municipal councils discovered, in
the course of its investigations, that in the pre-
paration of sausages it is customary to use the
bodies of unborn calves taken from slaughtered
cows.

Gov. Norman, of Queensland, left Sydney
for England, vid Maoriland, per Mararoa, last
Saturday. He was most popular, not only because
of his enviable record as a good and brave soldier,
but because he was unaffected and sincere, and
never stepped officially outside his groove.
Hanging is indeed a great deterrent!
Within a fortnight of Victoria’s last woman-
choking, three murders occurred, one of which
was in many respects similar to the crime of
Emma Williams. A desperate mother killed her
child and hanged herself immediately afterwards.
She had only learnt from the hanging not to leave
the job to society.
A huge crowd assembled at the funeral of
the Dacres, and many wreaths were sent —even by
people to whom the deceased were personally un-
known. The intense solicitude ever manifested
by Dacre for his wife, and the closeness with
which the ill-fated pair were apparently bound by
the memory of their dead child, seems to have
struck an especially deep note in the feminine
breast.
In a N.S.W. railway-carriage, the other
day, two handcuffed prisoners, a German and an
aboriginal, attacked their escort, an elderly con-
stable, and, after battering him almost to death,
changed their prison clothes and escaped from
custody with the policeman’s revolver and money.
At latest the German had been re-captured, but
the aboriginal was still at large with the coin and
pistol. Our countryman !

Hebrews of Australasia have enough talent and

Visitors to the Spouting Spring at Heli-
don, 70 miles from Brisbane, are always astounded
at the quantity of water they can consume at the
Spring. Ten or 12 tumblers in an hour or so are
taken with comfort. The reason is that the
tissues of the body are absorbing the water all the.
time. Something beneficial is always added to
the system by every glass of Helidon Spa water
consumed. Messrs. Tooth and Co., Kent
Brewery, are the Sydney agents.
Sir Henry Norman —Kanakaland’s retiring
Governor —is built something alter the pattern of ,
Abraham Lincoln. Not infrequently has he dis-
appointed lovers of flunkeydom by turning up at
an important function in a plain cab—a waggon-
ette at that. Healthy-minded, energetic, and
cosmopolitan, he will live in memory as one of the
most respectable figureheads Australia has yet
struck.

The Bulletin Christinas Number will
this year be published Dec. 12. It is a hard
matter to outdo previous Bulletin Christmas
Numbers in point of interest and variety, but if
that feat can be achieved it will be achieved on
Dec._ 12. The letterpress and illustrations are of
particularly high literary and artistic value, and
promise to move the great soul of the community
as that organ has rarely been moved before. The
price, as usual, is 7d. post free from this office to
any address ; and wholesale agents must positively
send in orders at once to ensure full supply.
HOW TO MAKE WO DLGROWING PAY.
The following extracts from “The National Wool
Company’s (Melbourne) Annual Review ” are
worthy of consideration by all engaged in the im-
portant industry of sheep shearing. The company
points out that there is a large increase in the pro-
duction of crossbred wools, and that a singular
carelessness is observable in the classing of these
wools. Various grades are mixed together, and
such clips are difficult to sell even at great sacri-
fices : stress is also laid on dipping immediately
after shearing. This important economy is
strongly recommended in order to kill all insects
such as ticks, which destroy the character of the
fleece besides injuring the health of the sheep (the
farmer loses a very large sum of money annually
by neglecting to dip the sheep). Deepskirting of
burry and seedy wools is also very necessary if
the farmer wishes to obtain a satisfactory sale for
his clip. Summing up the general outlook, the
National Wool Company points out that wool-
growing apd sheep-breeding will be more profitable
in the future if care and economy be devoted to
the management of the flocks. Rigorous culling
and the selection of the best sires is earnestly re-
commended. Consistently with its advice, the
National Wool Company has directed its
energies to securing economies in the marketing
of the great staple product. It has obtained
concessions in railway freights and is seeking
new wool-markets in Japan and China. Alto-
gether, the company is very active in progressive
movements, having for its object the extension
of the Australian wool-markets, •
A new pumping plant, which is gaining
every day in public favor, consists of a pump in
connection with a Capitaine Oil Engine. The
“ Capitaine,” being without boiler, is extremely
compact, and occupies only a few feet floor space.
It is well made, strong, but very light compared
with the ordinary engine and boiler. It is sim-
plicity itself, and is easily worked by anybody
without assistance of experienced engineers. The
engineis forwarded ready for work, may bemounted
anywhere at a cost of a few shillings, can be
easily started in five minutes, and then requires
no attendance whatever —in fact, may be left for
hours working by itself. Explosions cannot pos-
sibly happen, and the engine is absolutely safe in
every respect. The working expenses are very
low indeed, and are estimated not to exceed Ifd.
for raising 1000 gallons to a height of 100 ft. The
first cost of a complete pumping plant, pipes ex-
cluded, consisting of a 3h.p, engine and centri-
fugal pump capable of raising 8500 gallons per
hour 25 feet, is about .£l2O. For lifts above 40
feet force-pumps have to be used, which increase
the price by about £l5 to £25. Ex-Premier
Dibbs adopted the Capitaine engine when his
famous horse-pumping invention gave out.*
The big manufacturing firm, J. W. Cliff,
Jun., and Co., in Pitt-street,”between G.P.O. and
King-street, having purchased the stock and con-
nection of both Wallaoh’sand Newton and Lamb,
combines all the virtues of those long-standing and
highly-reputable firms with new virtues peculiarly
its own. The new firm’s shop-fronts’ display
would be an ornament to any business-city of the
globe, but what most distinguishes the firm is its
special lines ; the most modern styles of drawing-
room furniture—light, graceful, and just suited to
this climate ; also the bamboo cosy corner set —
light and handsome, and without a rival in
the Southern hemisphere; Bedroom suites are
also specialities. The firm being a manufacturer
guarantees its stock to be well seasoned ; the
workmanship A 1 ; the prices within easy reach
of the great general and lower than any
would-be rival’s. There is a big fitting, packing,
and polishing department; and the firm does a
big all-round Australian business, particularly
with Queensland and West Australia. It has
sent tons of stock Coolgardiewards. If the visitor
to Sydney finds time hang on his hands he
couldn’t do better than walk in to J. W. Cliff,
junr., and Co., sit in one of the fine new divan
chairs, and admire the charming effects that can
be arranged with artistic furniture.*

One provision of the
Vic. Malle Bill is that no
single owner shall hold more
than 1920 acres of mallee
land, and if a larger area
comes into his hands by in-
heritance or foreclosure of
a mortgage or otherwise, he
must, within five years, sell
sufficient to bring his estate
down to the 1920 acre limit.
This is a straight attempt to
prevent the accumulation of
big estates in the new coun-
try, and providing the
measure gets through the
Council it will be a most in-
teresting experiment. If it
succeeds the same provision
will almost in evitably be
applied sooner or later
throughout Victoria, and
the days of huge landlords
will be brought to a sudden
end.
DROUGHT .
“ 'Ad any breakfast this mornin'?”
"Norrer drop!"
it is astonishing what little hold
Separation has on some Northern Democrats.
Apparently incapable of comprehending how
vitally inseparable are the two principles of Demo-
cratic Government and Home Rule, they entirely
neglect to use a lever which, properly handled,
might have upset the Boodlewraith long ago.
The perpetual-leasehold clauses in the
Mai lee Bill last week transmitted to Victorian
Council provide for vermin-destruction, proper
fencing-, resumption by State on compensation,
fixing of rent by a board every 10 years at 2 per
cent, on unimproved land value. A reasonable
administration of these clauses should establish
the best land system in the world.
Frank Cotton, M.P. , regards non-fiscal
Australian Federation as “sheer cant:” “lam
a Federationist because I am a Freetrader.” And
Cotton is a Freetrader because he is a Single-
taxer, As one might say —“ lam a Trinitarian
because I am a Unitarian.”
Arthur Hunter Palmer, of Queensland, is
the only layman in Australia entitled to take the
place of a Governor when that individual resigns
or leaves the territory. And Griffith is the only
Chief Justice not possessed of the right of succes-
sion in such circumstances.
Major-General Hutton’s retirement has
given Premier Reid an opportunity of stating
that he does not favor the importation of Imperial
officers for the command of the N.S.W. forces.
But wait till Gov. Hampden arrives ! Govern-
ment House influence has been always directed
towards the absolute control by Imperial officers
of armed Australia, and Hampden in particular
has, long before he thought of coming here, urged
not only that the highest military office should
be filled by a British official, but that every militia
regiment in Australia should be commanded by a
man brought up at the heart of the empire.
McLean, hon. member of Vic. Cabinet*
raised the plea in the House that a universal lease-
hold system would mean universal robbery of the
State. The idea that the whole population would
find an advantage in repudiating their obligations
to the State, means, at the very worst, that the
people would pay themselves nothing. The whole
population being the creditor of each unit in the
matter of rent is a democratic ideal. The oppor-
tunity for all the population to oppress all the
units is an opportunity for a blessed form of des-
potism. At present, a few of the units oppress all
the rest of the population.
Hughes, Neild and other N.S. Wales
M’sL.A., in stipulating for proportional repre-
sentation in the Federal convention, go against
the one opinion which has been consistently held
by Australian Federationists since the question
was first raised. Even in Deas Thomson’s scheme
of 40 years ago each province was given an equal
number of delegates, although N.S.W. at that
time extended to the Gulf of Carpentaria, and
the population of South Australia was about the
same as that of Surry Hills to-day. Deas Thom-
son gave N.S.W., Victoria, S. A. and Tasmania
three members each, and the principle of the Reid
scheme, which gives ten each, is the same.
One of the strongest currents of opinion
set against Federation is noiselessly directed by
prominent officials in the public service of the
various provinces. The conviction of these is
that, under a Federation, the salaries of officials
in the individual States will be reduced, the signs
of the times all pointing to the unlikelihood of the
Federation paying its officers at increased figures.
Just as the bulk of the members of the various
Parliaments know that the Federal Parliament
will depress them to an almost aldermanic insig-
nificance, so the bulk of civil servants resH :
Federation their depression to the rank of borough
officials. A few civil servants will, of course,
expand into the higher sphere, but those who are
left behind will suffer manifest disadvantages.
For some time The Bulletin has con-
tinued to. call on Tory politicians and papers to
prove their good faith about the minimum- wage
principle. “ Protection is bad for the people, and
the minimum-wage is bad for the people—combine
these two (or three) evils and destroy them,” said
The Bulletin, or words to that effect. The
Argus some time since responded to this appeal,
and last week M'Kenzie and others of the same
species announced their conversion in Vic.
Assembly. Let the protective industries com-
plete their bargain, said M'Kenzie. So the whole
minimum wage was adopted for the Upper House
to maul if it dare. Protection may keep em-
ployment and wealth in a country, but it cannot
effect proper distribution of these advantages.
Protection is not the be-all and end-all of any
democratic creed. Oxygen is worse than useless
to put out a fire, and hydrogen has the same dis-
advantage ; but used together as water they form
the chief stand-by of every fire-brigade. The fire
that burns up the prosperity of the people needs
more than one element to subdue it.

In all probability an attempt will be made
Before the end of the session to overturn the
arner Ministry in Victoria, with the futile
M‘lntyre and the vacillating Shiels as the figure-
heads of the movement. If these two shadows
totter into power they ought to furnish the
most lamentable Ministry on record. Neither of
them can even lie down in his political policy
without holding on to something.
Henry Copeland’s proposal to represent
ecclesiasticism in the Federal Convention has
earned for him, in certain quarters, a reputation
for an excess of diplomatic cunning. Yet, if < one
good turn deserves another, the Anglican and R.C.
prelates ought to affectionately remember him
on the Convention polling-day, and who, in that
event, stands a better chance of being one of the
great Ten ?

F. : Madden, the prize jingo, lately raised
the foreign invasion howl some more, tak-
ing the riotous Russian for his bogey. The
depth of design in these displays of voluble
information on military subjects is all in keeping
with the Madden family’s, ambition to have the
first Australian peerage in its own circle. Who
is interested in keeping the Russians away ? The
property-hold-ers in Melbourne and Sydhey and
the banks. The bombardment of Melbourne and
Sydney would, do little real harm. Sydney wants
rebuilding, and Melbourne has too much office
accommodation as it is. The blockade of Sydney
and Melbourne would interfere with the export
trade, but that is the affair of the British creditor.
The democracy is not much interested in the
Russian invasion." 1 - In fact, the job of rebuilding
a razed city is the very thing that is wanted,
and a Russian entry into Melb., considered as a
spectacle, would give the Brassey affair 2000 yards
in a mile and win.

The sight of Mr. J. W. Hunt, of the Victorian Alliance,
writing to Mr. Longmore on a card covered with invita-
tions to try strong drink, is calculated to call up a smile
even upon the grim face of Mr. Higgins.
But suppose, instead of writing to Mr. Longmore,
he goes to see him by a railway which, to keep it
going, has to be subsidised to the extent of
.£350,000 a year out of the public revenue, which
is largely a drink revenue, said subsidy being
necessary because Mr. Hunt, when he goes to see
Mr. Longmore, is carried below cost price. Isn’t
that a ease in which Mr. Hunt, of the Vic.
alliance, reaps a direct benefit out of the Der-rink
Curse ? And isn’t that also calculated to call up
a smile on the face of Mr. Higgins ?

Personal Items.
The recent marriage of a statesman renders
inconsolable (so goes the story) a local policeman.
Rev. H. Gainford, of Parramatta Congre-
gational Church, is the fir'St to adopt the Yankee
fashion of a separate communion-cup for each
communicant. He fears the deadly microbe.
Jacob Montefiore, who died in England
Nov. 7, aged 9£, was the last survivor of the
original memlvers of the S.A. Association formed
in 1834 to bring about the colonisation of S.A.
It was 50 years on 26th Oct. last since
Hori Grey, after completing his terra as Governor,
sailed from Adelaide to Maoriland to begin his
first period of administration in that province.
At Gov. Brassey’s levde, Austria, France,
Russia and Germany were each represented by
one consul. But the black republic of Liberia
was represented by three consuls in circus uni-
form.
Capt. Guise, aide-de-church to Beeronet
Buxton, is as pious as his principal. On the pas-
sage of the Austral from London the Governor
read the lessons in the saloon, and the aide-de-
church went down to the third-class to preach.
Trenwith, the Yic. Labor leader, goes to
England, at the end of the session, to be treated
for cataract. He is now quite unable to read any-
thing, even with the most powerful glasses. It is
curious and pathetic that the two men who, in
the present session, have made the greatest strides
as debaters and political leaders, should both be
blind Trenwith in the Labor corner, and
M'Kenzie among th- Country party.
That old iden-
tity. W. B. Man-
tel!, M.L.C., who
lately died at the
age of 75, arrived in
Maoriland 55 years
ago, and was long
conspicuous in local
politics. To him
belongs the distinc-
tion of being the
boss land-speculator
of the century.
When Maoriland
was a Crown colony
he “bought” from
the natives thirty
million acres for
.£5OOO less than
one-sixth of a far-
thing per acre ! The
purchase was cou-
pled with certain
“concessions,”
which the natives
never received, and
the Secretary of
State refused to
ratify the wholesale
spoliation. Mantell
was distinguished
chiefly for his Joe
Millerisrns. It was
he who, going into
Bellamy’s and see-
ing Vogel in the act
of devouring a salad
remarked, “ How
doth the gentle Jew
descend upon the
green and tender
herb!”
Dr. Atkinson,
of Bendigo, is one
of the richest Vic-
torians. He has
been married six
times. A palmist
lately prophesied
that his present wife
would survive him
—not unlikely, see-
ing that he is close
on four-score,
though still hale
and hearty. His
station property is
considerable, and he
is a plucky mining-
speculator. He at-
tends hundreds of
lodge-patients and their big families for .£1 each
yearly, and has been seldom known to miss a 55.-
fee board-meeting. He has no time to drink or
smoke.
The Anglican beats the R.C. Church -in
the race for the first Australian-born bishop.
Cardinal Moran lias been hurrying affairs in that
direction lately, but the consecration of Rev.
“Jack ’’ Stretch at Melbourne makes first goal.
Gov. Brassey has two married daughters.
“Little Muriel,” of the Sunbeam, is now Lady
Cantelupe, daughter-in-law of Lady Delaware,
whose family takes root in a mediaeval George
Dean who tried to poison his uncle and bene-
factor.
The death (in England) is announced of
Mrs. J. Evelyn Liardet, who long resided in Ber-
rima, where her husband was C.P.S. under the
old regime. Mr. Liardet is a son of Liardet of
Liardet’s Beach, Victoria —the name by which
Saudridge was so long known.
There is quite a colony of Australians in
sleepy old Bruges, Belgium, where living is cheap.
Ex-Brigade-Major (or Colonel ?) Christie; ex-
Oouuty Court Judge Quinlan, late of Victoria;
the Camerons, of Tasmania ; Pearce, of Muswell-
brook or Singleton ; and others.
S.A. political amenities. Premier King-
ston to Grainger, M.P. : “You hit a blind man
with a stick from behind.” Grainger to King-
ston You presented Baker with a pistol that
would not go off.” Kingston to Grainger : “Your
remarks are only fit for a lying-in home.” And
so on.
A prisoner charged, before Q. Juflge Cooper
pleaded, in mitigation, that he forged a cheque
“after having had 17 or 19 whiskies before break-
fast.” His Honor: “Come now, you don’t
expeot me to believe that. I know something
about drinking myself.” There are no frills, even
on the Supreme Court, up North.
The Vic. Government is disappointed at
the failure of the King of Siam to purchase its
superfluous gunboats. His Majesty of Siam is,
by the way, an amphibious potentate who spends
most of his time, during the hot season, in a
specially-constructed glass pavilion, fitted with
air-pipes, at the bottom of a great tank in the
'palace gardens.
Dr. G. F. Belcher, of Dunedin, M.L., is
an even more uncompromising champion of Greek
and Latin study than Dr. Harris, late headmaster
of King’s School, Parramatta. Dr. B. actually
presented to the Australasian Association for the
Advancement of Science a paper on “ The Middle
Verb in Latin,” a thing whose existence very few
University men have ever heard of.
Old John M‘lntyre, the new leader of
Vic. Opposition, is 62, and almost the only thing
that can be said of him is that he was once a
chemist at Bendigo. He will probably be elbowed
out by-and-bye by the meteoric Shiels —if there
really is such a person. The curse of both
MTntyre and Shiels is that nobody really seems
quite certain if there is either of them.

viewer) is in appearance the most thoroughbred
Governor that Sydney has welcomed for several
years. A figure above middle height, slight and
well-built, with very small hands and feet, a low

A Maoriland paper tells this story about
Mantell, the lately deceased politician :
A thoroughfare in Wellington, in the olden time, was
known to the Maoris by a peculiar appellation which,
being anglicised, meaneth “ SanguiiKuy Ass-street.” It
was subsequently called Bowen - street. An English
tourist, hearing the story of the earlier name of the street,
asked’Mantell how it came to be .altered to Bowen-street.
Mantell, with a far-away look in his eye, replied : “ Out of
compliment to the Queen’s representative, but the senti-
ment implied in the name still remains the same.”
The well-beloved and brainy District Court
Judge Paul should by rights have succeeded
Judge Harding on Qi Supreme Court bench. But
his habits as a Bohemian raconteur are too well-
known, and “Sir Samuel” for the second time
decided that he “lacked the requisite dignity.”
As a hypnotist of undoubted powers, Paul has
exceptional opportunities of dealing successfully
with criminal cases. He is the author of the
First Offenders’ Act, which has already liberated
something over,a hundred juvenile criminals. It
was Judge Paul who, at Roma, once undertook to
hear—in the absence of an interpreter—an accused
Chinaman in his own defence. “ Man—yan—
yani—mok—a—hi—la—she—kor—she—Junee—a
—ma —mok—an —koy they John began
fluently. “Not so fast, please,” said Paul gravely
—pretending to take notes, “ I’m not a short-
hand writer.” Case dismissed.

sold a “show’’called the Paddington for a good

Candy is a first-class whip, and drives a spanking
pair of cobs tandem in the stre< N of Perth.

The story of Bill Ryan,
Bill, a storekeeper out Croydon
(Q.) way, was the local Her-
cules. One day a certain blocky
little politician (now in Westra-
lia) filled with real Mackay,
waited upon Mrs. Ryan and
spoke contumeliously of her
husband. “ He’ll bate yer,”
said Mrs. “I’ll go and
punch hitn in the eye,” replied
the politician. “Just you do,”
said Mrs. Ryan. The politician
went and did —he sailed up to
the great, big, good-natured
William and without warning
p ugged him right in the eye.
“Ye little baste!” said Bill
Ryan, catching him by the slack
of the trousers and the back of
the neck, and rubbing his nose
on the ground—“Ye would,
would ye ! ” Nor did William
stop until he had rolled the
politician’s nose flat and torn all
his clothes off. A couple of
weeks afterwards the politician
was able to get out of doors
again and met Mrs. Ryan in the
street. “Oi persave,” said Mrs.
Ryan, looking at his nose, “that
you did go to see my Bill.”

Dear Bulletin, — You missed the choicest
chunk of Fitz Gibbon’s poem :
With a kindly heart and an able hand,
And wisdom extracted from sea and land,
United with loveliness, charm, and grace,
To stand in our Sovereign Lady's place,
We welcome them all from the Sunbeam.
The bard’s reference to Queen Victoria in connec-
tion with the word “We” is an interesting
feature of his diabolical trenzy. —D.A.M.

possesses much literary talent, is a true poei

made many admirable verse-translations from
Goethe and others. Who was the lady in the
courtship days? ”
Some glaring recent incongruities re chemi-
cal analysis recall to a BuLLETiN-man an English
(Leicester) case, wherein a sample of coffee was
taken from a local grocer to the local analyst, who
certified to adulteration with roast acorns. The
grocer, however, had a sample analysed from the
sauie lot. The result was that it proved to be
pure, and the public analyst lost his appointment.
Every important analysis should be checked.

HALF-YEARLY REPORT of the Directors, with the Accounts for the Half-year to 15th April, 1895, presented to the
Proprietors at the Half-yearly General Meeting, held on Thursday, 3rd October, 1896.
BEPOET.
The directors submit to the proprietors the balance-sheet of the bank as at 15th April last, with the profit account
for the half-year ended on that date. After providing for rebate on bills current, for income tax, for colonial taxes on
note circulation, and for all bad and doubtful debts, the net profit for the half-year amounted to £40,854 2s. The
addition of £9641 os. 4d. brought forward from the previous half-year gives a disposable balance of £50,495 2s. 4d., of
which the dividend declared will absorb £40,000, leaving £10,495 2s. 4d. to be carried forward to the next account.
It is with regret that the directors'have to report that during the half-year to which the accounts relate the state
of business in the colonies was such as to seriously restrict advances and to diminish exchange profits. Since the close
of the half-year there has been a considerable recovery in the value of wool, and reports from the colonies indicate a
growing belief that the extreme point of the depression has been passed.
The bank’s business in Western Australia continues to progress, and confidence is felt in the future gold-production
of that colony. Further branches of the bank have been opened at Mensdes and Kalgoorlie.
To the very great regret of the directors, Mr. Edward Hamilton, the senior member of the board has resigned his
seat, after giving valuable and constant service as a director for upwards of thirty-live years. Mr. Charles Gipps
Hamilton is a candidate for the vacancy thus created, and the directors have much pleasure in recommending him to
the proprietors for election.
Since last meeting of the shareholders the directors have had the satisfaction of personal conference with the
superintendent, Mr. Sawers, who recently paid a visit to this country, and is now on his way back to Australia. They
feel assured that benefit will accrue to the bank’s interests from the personal interchange of views between the board
and their chief representative in the colonies.
The dividend declared is at the rate of 5 per cent, per annum, or £1 per share for the half-year, and it will be
payable, free of income tax, in London and in the colonies on the 4th of October.
E. M. NELSON, Chairman.
4 Threadneedle-street, London, 26th September, 1895.

How Women Differ.
Although “ all roads lead to Rome,” each route
has its peculiar characteristics, advantages, and
adherents. This applies equally in all phases of
life. While ladies may differ on questions as to
methods of comfort, pleasure, and enjoyment,
those who have had any personal experience to
guide them agree on one important subject: they
are unanimous in admitting that the best, shortest
route, and safest method, to ensure a prompt re-
turn to health is to have immediate recourse to
Warner’s Safe Cure whenever danger signals,
which intimate the approach of any deviation to
good health, appear. The following expression of
gratitude from a previous sufferer is enough to
guarantee satisfaction to other ladies who may
wisely decide to follow her example. She says:
“Three years ago I was suffering deeply from
some complaint which the doctors described as
acute dyspepsia, under which I was rapidly
wasting away. My appetite became fickle, and
what little I did eat was not retained on the
stomach. I was subject to dizziness, severe head-
aches, and irregular action of the bowels. From
lack of energy and want of ability to exert my-
self and increasing weakness, life became almost a
burden. Notwithstanding that various doctors
attended and prescribed I obtained no benefit,
but gradually lost in weight and strength. I be-
came in the end so reduced that a friend from
Beecliworth could scarcely recognise me. This
same person advised me to have recourse to War-
ner’s Safe Cure. Thereupon I commenced to take
it, keeping to the directions laid down in their
pamphlet. I used in all twenty-five bottles, and
during the whole time improved in health,
strength, and weight, so that by the time the last
bottle was finished 1 had recovered four stone of
my weight, and was, in fact, entirely cured.”
Mrs. Margaret Lopouple, Landlady Club Hotel,
Benalla, Viet., gives her experience in the honest
belief that it is a duty she owes her sex.*
HOW TO AVOID THE INJURIOUS
effects of stimulants. —The present system of
living—partaking of too rich foods, as pastry, sac-
charine, and fatty substances, alcoholic drinks,
and an insufficient amount of exercise—frequently
deranges the liver. I would advise all bilious
people, unless they are careful to keep the liver
acting freely, to exercise great care in the use of
alcoholic drinks, avoid sugar, and always dilute
largely with water. Experience shows that
porter, mild ales, port wine, dark sherries, sweet
champagne, liqueurs, and brandies are all very apt
to disagree ; while light white wines, and gin or
whisky largely diluted with soda water will he
found the least objectionable. ENO’S “FRUIT
SALT ” is peculiarly adapted for any constitu-
tional weakness of the liver; it possesses the
powers of reparation when digestion has been dis-
turbed or lost, and places the invalid on the right
track to health. A world of woes is avoided by
those who keep and use ENO’S “FRUIT SALT,”
therefore no family should ever be without it.
Sold by all chemists and stores.*
At Whangarei (M.L.) is a hill capped by
four curiously-shaped rooks. The largest, seen
from the east, shows a scarred but pronounced
profile of a man, like a well-tattooed Maori. The
others resemble a wahine with a child pick-a-back,
trudging in an opposite direction with two picca-
ninnies following tier. The Maoris in the vicinity
had a tradition that the largest rock was the spirit
of a famous rangatira called Mauaia, and the
other rocks were his wife and children. A mis-
sionary once attempted to ridicule this belief out
of an ancient Maori, but was met in this way :
“Maori believe in Manaia. He made of rook.
Pakeha believe in Lot’s wife. She made of salt.’’
“I ’member when Mat Lynch, then just
arrived from Ireland, ivas green—green as a frog.
Me an’ him was mates that time. One afternoon
we struck Pat Grady’s pub., toot o’ Cherrytree
Hill, ’bout 25 miles this side of Wallerawang.
After a time we goes for a stroll troo’ the orchid,
when I sights a old-man kangaroo up agin’ a
apple tree. I goes an’ borrows Grady’s rifle.
* Holy saints ! ’ sez Mat, ‘ are yer goin’ to shoot
apples off the thrae ?’ ‘ Apples, be hanged,’ sez I,
‘ there’s a kangaroo ! ’ Mat never seen a kangaroo
before—he didn’t see him then. I banged an’
just missed —then away bounded the old fellow.
Mat seen him then. ‘Well done, Mac., me frind,’
sez Mat, ‘ yer blew his two front legs off ! ! S’elp
me, Bill, if that idiot didn’t go lookin’ for them
two legs ! ” —Aloz.
The cussedness of inanimate objects again.
The road from Dunedin (M.L.) to the reservoir—
a place where the traffic, except on holidays, is
about twenty persons per day—runs for part of
the way between high steep banks. One day-
recently a young lady was passing this spot when
a boulder fell on her head and killed her. No one
remembers any falls of rock there before. On an
average, probably, there was some one underneath
that boulder for about five minutes in each 24
hours, and even then the chances of its landing right
on the head of the passer-by were, perhaps, one in
thirty or so. Yet after waiting for over twenty
years since the road was constructed this particu-
lar rock managed it. The young lady was a
stranger and had never been there before, and
the boulder had, perhaps, been waiting for that
chance since before the Pyramids were built.
THE AUSTRALIAN GEM TRADE.
Undoubtedly the largest buyer of opals in the
rough state is H. Newman, the well-known Mel-
bourne jeweller, who has been connected with
this branch of the gem trade since its inception,
and keeps a large staff of skilled lapidaries busily
employed on these and other Australian gems.
He is also a large buyer of pearls and diamonds,
and gets through an immense quantity of Queens-
land sapphires. The firm has been established
for over 40 years, and has a reputation for straight
dealing which is sufficient guarantee to con-
signors of rough gems that they will be honorably
dealt with. The establishment is still at the old
spot in Elizabeth-street, right opposite the Post
Office, and is well worthy of a visit of inspection.*
From time to time there has been talk in
N.S.W. judicial circles about abolishing juries.
Now the public talks openly of abolishing indivi-
dual Judges and insisting upon all important
criminal trials being held before two Judges
instead of one.

T. JAQUES MARTIN, General Manager.
There is much pleasant gossip in Mrs.
Paton’s “Sketches from the New Hebrides.”
The lady describes how one islander was accus-
tomed to come to church attired solely in a snow-
white vest, while another usually came with a
cotton skirt pinned round his neck. On the birth
of Mrs. Paton’s little girl the native women con-
doled warmly with the mother over the fact that
it wasn’t a boy. Herein civilised and barbarous
prejudices are again at one.

The "Schoolmarster."
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The bush brain-driver is an absolute monarch if
firm ; if otherwise, a serf. I fought hard for the
reins.
They were wild youngsters; I left them at
Xmas ; returned to re-open on Jan. 19; it took me
three days to run 11 down, two more to bring
them in. (Liz. Kelly is out yet; I found some
of her red hair on one snare, but no further
traces).
Jim Baker was a “lad.” He “ was seein’ how
far he could throw a little stone, sir,” and laid
Tom Drummond low. I quilted Jim, Jim,
senr., quilted me ; nine stone seven cannot argue
against 13 odd. Jim held father’s coat.
Aaron turned his rod into a snake, the magi-
cians ditto ditto. Aaron’s swallowed them, and
once more became a hickory. Dan Ryan wants
to know “Did them other fellers get their sticks
back ? ”
I board at Mrs. Nolan’s. Calves and chickens
are prosaic society. She was prosaic ; lung years
of drudgery had blotted “ Romance ” from outlier
soul.
Maud was pretty—and 18. I—a “gilded-
youth ” of 19—and city-reared.
What more natural?—but Mrs. Nolan was
prosaic—and watchful.
t Scene : The Wattle-and-Dab. Tea-time. The
“ old man ” carves ; she pours out the tea. Her
chair is opposite my gum-block. (N.B. —Chairs
are scarce up here.) Silence reigns.
1 Class-supervising cultivates a special accom-
plishment, it increases one’s visual compass. I
have the table under control while fossicking that
fly out of my cup—and two pairs of furtive eyes
are embarrassing.
Desperation forces me to break the silence :
“That Durham of yours looks poor, Mr. Nolan ”
—I can talk “ cattle ” well, now, but Nolan’s
logic wins—he enjoys my discomfort.
She coughs. I translate it; our eyes meet —the
mother’s. More cough, plenty handkerchief and
heightened color.
“ What’s the matther, Mor-rd ? ”
“Hem—a—hem; I swallowed a crumb the
wrong way, mother.”
Desperation works me. “ How are your fowls,
Mrs. Nolan ? ” I’ve learned to talk “ fowl,” too.
“ Oh, thank ye, sorr, me fowls is well, but I’m
afeerd of a hawk afther me chickens.’ I collapse.
Maud was a nice girl— too good for Jim
Burnett. Elsie Burnett is my pupil now. Time
flies.
Wesfcralia.

Dear Bulletin,— A "Poverty Point” par. remarks
that "It doesn't look well to s°e a mesmerist’s subjects
hanging round the stage-door.” Surely there is no longer
any Iv-lief that mesmerists’ subjects are amateur volun-
teers? The poor devils are, in almost every case, hired to
come upon the platfo m and make objects of themselves.
The “ Professor ” tests them beforehand, finds out how
he can heat use them—as singing fools, laughing fools,
acting fools, or what not—and puls them through a re-
hearsal. All the same, they are, more or le.-s, mesmerised
when they give their perform voces. A number of them
are formed into a concert-party, for instance, and the
Prof-ssor will say to each in turn, " What song will you
sing, sir?” Some of the replies suggest that the victims
hadn’t decided what they would sing before they came
ttiere, hut the "star" vocalist and most experienced sub-
ject of the lot will give the name of his song in a sulky,
suspicious way, which impliesQarn. you know what
my song is well enough.” All the same he is hypnotised,
consciously hypnotised, and he wouldn't sing half as
ridiculously if he were in a healthy stale of mind. Some-
times these fellows seem indignant against the Professor
on r-covering their senses ami perceiving their degrada-
tion, and sometimes their feeble hursts of anger are
genuine. They may have had the Pr- fessor’s promise
not to degrad - them too much. But often it is mock
anger part of the business. Ther ’a a lot of humbug
m ogled with the genuineness of such beastly "enter-
tainments.” Id's hard for an outsider to guess where the
mesmerist’s p >wer ends and the subject’s hired volition
commences. Indeed, the operator doesn't exactly know’
and tne "subject” is incapable of settling the point. In
its way it is an interesting point-beastly interesting.—

Mrs. Bancroft has been telling her love story to a
young, confiding little female periodical known as Home
Chat. Mrs B.’s narrative sounds simple, or otherwise,
just according to the readers age and knowledge of
an -ient theatrical history. She first met her Bancroft,
she says, at a Liverpool theatre, where she treated him
rather coolly, because he was reputed to be a lady-killer.
Next time she went to Liverpool she acted in several
pieces with him, and was so much impressed that she
never quite forgot him afterwards. “ When I was plan-
ning to be a manageress,” says the demure old ladv, "I
thought I couldn’t have a cleverer or handsomer young
actor in my company than he, so he was engaged ; and
then—a long lime after that, though —we were engaged.
And that is the end of the story.” Mrs. Bancroft
reminiscing of her spinsterhood and "engagement” to
B. “a long time after” is funny enough, hut not half as
droll as the noiio i of a lady-repvrler going to interview
her on the delicate subject. By the way, there is no
mention of the Bancroft fix, who hears such a strong
resemblance to the Roval family.

Madden, who is studying singing under Marotnsi, in

much of her. Critics who have heard her say th it if she
adopted the stage as a profession she would be hound to
make a great reputation. But she is only taking up
singing as a superior accomplishment.

Clown Harry Payne, the last survivor of the Payne
pantomimids, died the other day. The Yokes family
also is extinct, but some of the Lauris remain on earth,
The fame of the Lviris and Paynes will certainly live as
long as Gilbert’s Bali Bdlads, because the Bishop of Rum-
ti-Foo, before sailing forth to his savage see, took lessons
in “ the Payne and Liuri feat." And, in another ballad,
he educated his flick tip to the new style of dancing—
“for he thought the choicest fruits of progress ought to
bless the negro’s home.”

SPORTING NOTIONS.
There ia one member of past Australian Elevens
who haa never been seen drunk, but who has swallowed
more juice than any two other Australian cricketers com-
bined. Which is a large order. This Australian would
take a couple of friends on about 8 o’clock, drink them
asleep hy nine, move round for more fight for five minutes
or so; pick up a couple more friends, drink them into a
charge of drunk anil disorderly and tearing uniform by
ten; bear off on another tack, fall in with two or three
more, and drink them blind, deaf and dumb by 11 ; then
meet other mates coming out of the music-halls, take
them home, drink all hut the teetotaller of the crowd into
delirium tremenx, and knock out that teetotaller by
sitting up till 6 a.m. After this he would mutter some-
thing about constitution, not vice or virtue, miking a
man happy ; go to bed, sleep a lew hours, and get up in
time to make a rattling score.

S. A, is the champion province for turning out
faded and worn-out cricket veterans in big matches. The
only new man in the match against Vic. was daxt'di, who
played in big matches 10 years ago, and is believed
to have arrived in S.A., at a great age, in 1806.
There are mmy young players in Adelaide who have been
performing well for years, notably Bailey, Chinner, Green,
and Travers, hut as long as some of the old players can
crawl about on crutches they are certain to be picked for
years to come. Vic. and N.S.W. have long since found
out the mistake of pursuing this policy, and S.A. ought
to profit bv their examnle.

Cricketer Coningham is now, although a
chemist, working for the Graziers Butchering Co., Bris-
bane. That firm snaps up any active crick her of note,

mainly responsible.

essayed a record. The school-whistle saved Clem. Hill
(or Laver?). Tnis ia a true bill.—M. F. Darcby, Dandar-

It now appears that Onward’s “surprise ”in the
V.K.C. Handicap, the other Saturday, was a droll inci-
dent altogether. Tne Bal arat coll was start, dto oblige
tlie Palo Alto people, who wanted him to in ike a pace for
their slayer. Onward’s jockey, however, made the pace
only moderate for the first half mile ; then, starting with
a good lead, put in a fast mile and a quarter, and heat
them all. If the riders of either Palo Alto, Waterfall or
Burrahari had been keen judges of speed they would
have put on some steam in the early part of th- race.
Xiien Onward would, undoubtedly, have gnt run down at
the finish. Dawes, on Burrahari, showed bad Judgment,
seeing that the big horse—who was carrying very little
more than his Melh. Cup weight—allowed the time for
this If miles to he 7sec. slower, in proportion, than the
time for the two-mile Cup, in which Burrahari ran a good
third. Mistakes of this kind upset racing “ form ” in
long distance races, much more frequently than had luck
prevents the best horse from winning. Given a suffi-
ciently strong pace all the way, there’s not much fear of
the best horse being unable to vet through his field
towards the end of a 14 furlong struggle—that is, of
course, if he hasn’t been previously lamed or knocked
down at one of the curves.

The futile stupidity of the N.S.W. anti-gaming
laws has been vividly silhouetted once more by the
reply of N.S.W. Attorney-General Want to C. G. Heydon,
M.L.C., his occasional alter ego, to the effect that he
knew that Sydney landed properties were being b'tteried,
but believed that they were being lotteried in Queens-
land ; also, that if there was any contravention of N.S.".
law he would immediately slop it. That is not enough.
If Jack Want would be thoroughly logical, he should at
once inform the Kanakaland Premier that this lottery
constitutes " an unfriendly act,” and, after the lapse of
certain days of grace, he should, declare war against
Queensland.

Sirgent, in Vic. Leg. Council ; “Sweeps had a
more demoralising effect oil the community than even
street betting. He could give an instance that came
under bis own nodce. He employed a number of men,
and one year several of them invested in a sweep and won
£3O. That demoralised them to a certain extent. The
following year they went into another sweep, and eight
of them drew close on £l2OO. The effect of that win was
demoralising to I lie who.e community. Nearly every
person in Warragul was bitten with the belling mania.
The popular type of racer is the mulish, with
lumpy liddle-head, but Auraria retferts to the old-
fashioned, giactful, rakish Blink Bonny.

Tom Guild, who, with his two sons, shares in
the first prize in the St. Alban’s lottery, is one of three
brothers (Scotchmen) who settled in Seymour, Vic., in
the early days and there carried on store and hotel-keep-
ing and grazing as partners. They got rich and divided
the assets, Tom taking the store. After the division the
other two bid hers continued to pro>p >r, but Toni’s good
nature and generosity, as well as misfortune (writes a
Bulletin correspondent), have kept him in harness right
up to now. He lias a large family, and his eldest
daughter is a girl of great beauty. They are among the
niosl popular people in the district, and it is pleasant to
see Fortune for once award a favor with her eyes open.

Cyclist Harris, just arrived from Eng., is a
professional under Hie rules which govern English
cycling. On one occasion lie I ode for a £ 5 note instead
of a marble clock, and was thereupon disqualified as an
amateur. In Australia he will 1 not be hampered by this
distinction, as all prizes are very sensibly given in cash.
Australian tracks may be bad, but a Winning wheelist
always gets full value for bis ride.
A. W. Harris, the Englishman, should make
things hum on our cycling tracks. As a sprinter tie is
Slid to be more like Zim than any other rid. r. His best
timeg are Quarter-mile, 27 15th sec,; one mile, 1 min.
58 3 slh sec.; 5 miles, 11 min. 35 1 sth sec. ; 10 miles (on
grass), 27 min. 23 4-slb sec.—all records, w ith the excep-
tion of the mile, which has just been reduced to 1 min.
55 sec.

An excited host of 30,003 Sydney Cricket-
ground record—last Saturday greeted Zimmerman ; and
N.S.W. Wheelmen’s League, having spent £9OO, reaped
£l4OO in “gate.” Neither Zim nor his manager ever saw
so great and vociferous a gathering. Its weight broke
through the grand-stan fence in several places ; once
among it there was no escape. For his heat in the Aus-
tralian Half-Mile Championship, the long-legg'd, long-
haired "Aalhah” came out (with his trainer wheeling
the bike), and, in short black i a"ts and blue Jersey,
walked to the starting-post. Buik (S. Aus.) and M’lnnes
(N.S. W.) started with Zim, and the three on each
other at hearse-like gait. When it came to racing there
was none, for the two might as well have tried to catch a
swallow us race Zim home.
But the sensation was to come. So far, the
crowd had recognised Zim’s legs as great legs, but still
legs. Parsons (Vic.), Lewis (N.S.W.), Iredale (Vic.), and
Zim wailed for the final's pisiol ; Zim. on the outside.
Flash I and away flashed Parsons, Lewis nipping in
straight on Parsons’ hind wheel, Iredale third, Zim.
fourth, and still outside—worst position, worst running.
Thus round they sped the first lap. Three-fourths of a
lap from home Parsons forced the battle at his top ;
Lewis answer*d, then Iredale, then Zim. just enough to
keep “thyar.” Two hundred yards from home Zimmer-
man picked up his machine and shot out like a
meteor; he just whizzed down that track leaving a
singed line for his paralysed rivals to follow'. He made
common hacks of the representative Australians, heating
them by 40,vds. in that 200 finish. Then couldn’t have
beaten third-raters like it. Ttie three Auslialiar s came
home in a hunch, but Parsons was second, and Iredale
just beat Lewis.
On that form, bar accidents, Zim. must win
every race he starts for in Australia.
Matthews (Vic.) disappointed onlookers in the
Sydney Wheel Race. Perhaps he lacks judgment.
Nevertheless, he’s worth watching : he couldn't ride
slow enougli to win Ids heat in the League Cup.
Harkins, the youngster who started at 60 to 1
on the 240 yards’ mark, easily won the Sydney Wheel
Race (6100). In ids heat he made his own pace and
actually gained on Portoa(ltaly), the scratch mnn. In
the final he again made Ids pace and came in 30 yards
ahead of a “ busted” field.
Elliott, the talked-of dead-bird, rode in fine
style in his heat for the Sydney Wheel Race, hut d ed
away from sheer exhaustion in the final. He hasn’t the
strength to win a hard two-mile race.
Megson was snuffed-out in bis heat for the
Half-Mile Australian Championship. Iredale (Vic.) and
Martin (America) were against him, and Iredale did the
right thing in taking the lead and sprinting a lap and a
quarter from home. That iong-sustaim d sprint was too
mu di for Megson, who came at the finish with desperate
unaxailingness.
Portoa (Italy) and Martin (America) showed
themselves to he real game pluggers, hut Parsons, Meg-
son and Iredale were all too fast for them. It is said that
these nmn are nol used to the grass-track. But Zim beat
Australia as he liked on that same grass-track.
Megson’a gameness was, next to Zim’s wonder-
ful win, the feature of last Saturday’s cycling. In the
First-Class Handicap (mile and a half) he beat Parsons
(Vic.), Martin (America), Portoa (Itab ). Bulk (S. Ans.',
O’Hrien (N.S. W,), Payne (N.S. W.) am) Chapman (Banana-
land). Payne, Mgson’s stab e male, blocked Parsois;

was, Megson was bir d 10J yarns Loin home ; n-vert.hr*
le she heat Parso by a f -xv inches. Megson fainted
afterwards—it was said through over-training.

and fitted-up for the purpose. The management provides
for its patrons tweed blouses ard knickerbockers —a
costume held to he superior to skirts for lean ing, at any
rate. The attendants at present are men, though ladies
are promised by-and-bye.

could have for attacking Australia so long as Aus-
tralia minds her own business?

Dr. Richardson, an English expert, says the
effort of driving a bicycle, if persisted in to a certain ex-

duced hy alcohol; and Dr. Springihorpe, of Melh., calls 1

and thb swelled head of the female rider is fully accounted

Huret, the Frenchman who holds the 24 hours
record (515 miles odd), is a cycling phenomenon, being
83 years of age. The average cyclist begins to go off at
22 or 23, and even the great Zim., at 26, is a comparative
greybeard among wheelmen. In the first flight of Aus-
tralian racing men scarcely one is over 23; Parsons is 2c,
Megson 21, Turner 22, IC<ihle 21, Pither 22. and O'Brien
82, Johnson, who is chasing Zim. from ’Murka, is 21.
