• * • *
Certain well-known Sydney thieves can bo seen
daily sprint-running, one with the other, to keep them-
selves in form to get away from the “ Johns.”
In the bygone halcyon days of pedesttianism
the thieves were regular goers on the track. It was a
hard tussle for a runner to keep his clothes. One dandy
ped. used to always bring ids clothes out and nut them
down beside the winning-post, and then walk warily
backwards down to his mark, keeping his eyes on Ids
togs. “Straight wire” this! He didn’t raise a row
because, if tie had, he’d have been found next morning
with Ids throat cut. Even now, if anyone wants an
enemy waylaid and partly or wholly extinguished, he can
engage an x-ped. for the transaction with a “ quid” or so.

The latest in trotting sulkies is one in which
the wheels, looked at from the side, seem to be under the
horse’s belly, while the seal is just above his loins. The
wheels are a.so only the size of those on a safety-bicycle,
and are pneumatic-tyred.

• * * *
If N.S.W. Parliament passes—as it probably
will —the leasing proposal in the Fisheries Amending Act,
recommended by the Commission, 90 per cent, of the
licensed fishermen (writes “Ponte ” to The Bulletin) may
put all their gear aboard and take the plug out of their
boats in 50 fathoms of water. They may also ballast
themselves with a copy of the report and go down with
their boats, for the most of them can do nothing else
hut fish. If the leasing idea becomes law every fisher-
man must fish on ids own ground week after week. Now,
there is not the slightest doubt that fish are at all times
migratory. Their movements are extremely erratic, and
arise from various causes, the chief being the season,
wind, weather, and state of the water. Consequently,
the men on one shore might make 50 hauls for 50 fish,
while those on the opposite shore kill more than they
can get to market. Tne men can’t make a living as it is.
What chance will they have if confined to one hauling-
ground ? It is always necessary to follow the fish as
they shift, and as a matter of justice the men must have
equal rights to every ground subject to priority only.
Then, again, any man with a water-frontage will be aide
to take out a license and get a lease —which he will do
for the sake of his line-fishing. And here you have tire
key to the whole tiling. Fatinan wishes to preserve Ids
little hit of pleasure at the cost of the.“ other fellow’s ”
livelihood. The fishermen look to Farnell, M.P., for
better things, hut if he allows this to beoora- law he will
be more execrated than the inspectors under the present
Act—words can say no more.

Melb. : There was really little enthusiasm
over Brassey’s reception. Compare it with Hope-
toun’s departure, a real downright outbreak of
stupid effervescence, the despair qf a reformer,
not to say a revolutionist. An elderly gent and
his young wife are handicapped versus the Hope-
touns, and, besides, the fuss is only over what they
are going to do.
One of the occasional joys of the Cool-
gardie district is the “ bad camel.” Recently an
animal of this description got a man down, bit
him considerably, and rubbed him systematically
in the dust. Then it picked up another
man by the leggings and threw him 10
yards or so ; chased a digger into a patch of scrub
and kept him there for some time ; and finally
walked up the street and spat on people pro-
miscuously. The bad camel is almost the only
gleam of humor in that profoundly serious
country.
That Port Darwin police-court case, in
which one Gully was fined “for encouraging a
policeman in idleness, while on duty, by allowing
him to play a game of draughts in his shop,”
gives unmistakable proof of the influence of the
Mongolian mind on the legal methods of that
Chow-haunted portion of Australian territory.
It is an essentially Chinese practice to seek to
discover behind an offender some person indirectly
connected with the offence, and, when found, to
treat him as a principal. Thus, at Canton the
other day, a man who murdered his father was
executed—and so was the murderer’s school-
master, for not having instilled better principles
into his pupil’s mind.
For coughs, colds you can’t beat Colemane
and Sons Eucalypte Extract.*
N.S.W. Chief Justice Darley, the other
day, refused to her mother and paternal grand-
mother the custody of an illegitimate girl, aged
between 13 and 14, whom her deceased father had
placed in a North Sydney Convent. Judges
Windeyer, Simpson and Cohen, sitting in Banco,
have reversed this decision, and the child is to be
given to her mother. Judge Windeyer gave a
learned deliverance, in which the doctrine was
affirmed that the mother of an illegitimate child
has the same rights over her child as the father of
a legitimate one has over his; that in the case of
boys up to 14, and girls up to 16, the parent’s
wishes must prevail, unless a material, and not
merely a sentimental disadvantage to the child,
can be shown to be a probable result; and that,
unless grave reasons can be shown for a change,
the parent’s right of custody continues until the
child is 21. Which appears to be uncommonly
good sense, as well as good law.
Dinner-table at a Launceston (Tas.) hotel.
Heavy Johnny—recent arrival from ’Ome : “ Took
a run down to Port Arthur whilst over Hobart
way, but can scarcely believe the stories I heard
about the old convict days, or that so many of the
good fellows I have met in the Island are de-
scended from convict parents.” Sydney Civil
Servant —over for a holiday: “Why not? Take
my case, tor instance. My pa and his brother
belonged to a good English family. Hard-up,
they advertised in the Times— ‘ Situation vacant
for a decent man.’ Applicants were decoyed in
a boat on the Thames, knopked on the head,
pockets rifled, and bodies anchored with a hun-
dredweight of stone round their necks. After
disposing of about 14, they had the bad luck to
chuck one out at high-water on a sand-bank, and
the body was discovered when the tide ran out.
Pa got a fright and turned Queen’s evidencp.
Uncle was hanged, and pa got 14 years’ transpor-
tation to Tasmania, where he founded one of the
first families. Am Ito blame for that ?” A roar
of laughter from all round the table here gave the
show away, and when Johnny saw how the land
lay, he left the table in a huff.

wages. Manager (to new kitchen-hand who has
been getting over big spree): “ What makes your
hand shake so, Mike?” (He had signed his name
across three lines). Mike: “Faith, sir, Oi’m
always that way when in the prisence of a gintle-
man.” Mike didn’t get the sack that week.

Special Number of The Bulletin. The

Melb. Argus is afc great pains to show that
it will pay the Victorian farmer much better if
his crops only average five bushels per acre, in-
stead of seven, which latter figure is the forecast
average. In the latter case, the Freetrade organ
points out,_ there will be a surplus for exportation,
and the price will be ruled by the London stan-
dard. In the event of there being only five
bushels, however, that will just meet the local
demand, and the farmer will be able to command
a much higher price. But the Argus does not
advise the farmer to consider this view of the
question with the object of diversifying his out-
put, and limiting his wheat-crops whilst culti-
vating sugar-beet, oil seeds, &c., &c., for which
there is a big local market. Wheat-growing for
export is only another name for beggary, and
until the local supply of tobacco, sugar, hops, and
a lot of other things readies the demand, the con-
stant struggle to tear grain out of the ground and
pay 16,000 miles of freight upon it to an already
over-crowded market is sheer waste.
A CONFERENCE OF PREMIERS.
The august heads of the parliaments of Austra-
lia met in serious conclave to decide important
questions of the day. One by one the various
subjects were discussed and settled in the usual
quick way of a conference, without thought of
what would be the opinion of the individual Par-
liaments when the matter is laid before them.
Federation, Customs-duties, Governors’ salaries,
Postal Unions, Dog-taxes, &0., were all agreed
upon with the greatest equanimity until the ques-
tion of the import duty on tobacco and cigars was
reached. Commenting on the large importation
of Frossard’s Cavour Cigars, some of the clever
heads were for increasing the duty, others thought
they were such a boon that the duty should be
removed altogether; and, after a great deal of
discussion, it was decided to leave things un-
changed, as, by lowering the tariff, the revenues
would lose a big item, while, by increasing it, the
public would be deprived of a cheap and harmless
enjoyment, the retail price of Is. for a packet of 8
placing it within the reach of all lovers of a good
weed.*
The Sydney clergy, elated with their suc-
cess as rain makers, have opened out in a new
line. The trousseau of an expectant bride was
recently stolen, and in the evening all hands
joined in a special prayer-meeting for the recovery
of the missing property. The lady (evidently a
doubter) had just bought a fresh outfit, when she
was sent for to identify her property, discovered
in a pawnshop. The misadventure delayed the
marriage by just half-an-hour.

With an ordinary tape measure determine the width
round the joints AA, round the instep 88, round heelCC,
round the ankle DD ; next ascertain the length and width
of tread by placing the foot on a sheet of paper, and
running a lead pencil round the heel and toes. Cut the
outline thus made with a scissors, and enclose with the
other measurements, stating whatsizeyou irenerally take,
what are your peculiarities— a weak ankle, perhaps a pet
corn, ingrowing nails, or a blooming bunion. All these
little matters attended to. If you have a long thin foot, or
a short thick one, with very high instep, send to ELLIS’S,
where you are sure to get a fit. The proprietor, with a
view of introducing his new and simple system into the
country districts where practical boolmaking is unknown,
has decided to supply his patrons at the lowest cash price.

Parents, Please Note this Fact.
The Manager, Mr. W. P. Morrison, is sparing
neither care nor trouble to see to the performances
being such as will be high-toned and respectable
in the extreme. Naught that the most fastidious
can cavil at. The order of the hour is that the
anmstment shall be of a healthy and moral
character —a place whereto the husband can bring
his wife and daughters, ora brother his sister, and
know that they are in safe keeping and at a feast
of rollicking fun in songs, dances, sketches, acro-
batic feats, etc., etc.

San Francisco, the wickedest, is also the
most chivalrous of cities. Its police-commissioner
has ordered that the officers who lead unprotected
ladies over the dangerous crossings must in future
wear white cotton gloves lo prevent the perspira-
tion from their hands soiling the delicate silks
under their protection. Next, please.
Cole’s Book Arcade now runs from the centre
of the block in Bourke-street to the centre of the
block in Oollins-street, Melb., a distance of 660 ft.,
and contains the following 17 departments, mostly
literary and artistic : 1, New Books; 2, Second-
hand Books ; 3, Stationery; 4, Music; 5, Fancy
Goods; 6, Toys ; 7, Perfumery ; 8, Pictures ; 9,
Artists’ Materials; 10, an Ornament Exhibition ;
11, a Japanese Exhibition ; 12, a Wonderland ;
13, a Smiling Gallery; 14, a Child’s Picture
Gallery ; 15, a Bargain Room ; 16, a Tea Room ;
17, a splendidly arranged new Wholesale Book
Department, containing half a million books. The
main walks of the establishment are half a mile
long. •
London, Oct. 24.
Pope Leo XIII. has convened the Sacred College of
Cardinals with the view of consulting with his political
council as to the desirability of pronouncing the temporal
power an article of faith.
If I remember correctly (and memory without cor-
rectness is not memory) the spiritual and temporal
authority of Rome were supposed rewards given
by the Emperor Constantine for the cure of a case
or leprosy in that potentate, simply as a mark of
gratitude to no one in particular. The decretals
and donation were long afterwards proved to have
been forgeries. Hallam says of the decretals
(giving one or other of the powers) : “ Upon these
spurious decretals was built the great fabric of
Papal supremacy, a fabric which has stood when
its foundations have crumbled.” I make no com-
ment, but I have a bet to Iay.— SCOTTY THE
W HINKLER.
The most monstrously absurd feature of
the China-Russia alliance scare was a cable
announcement, from a Parisian paper, that
“ Emperor William of Germany has promised to
support Russia in the East.” In the first place,
William isn’t an irresponsible potentate, who can
back up any sort of promise with the force of the
German army. In the next place he is personally
bound to be as anti-Russian in sentiment as the
people he “rules.” When any matter of Great
Britain v. Russia threatens to bring about a war
the Germans favor the easy and accessible island
which so many of them have adopted. For one
German who learns the Russian language and
goes to Russia “on the make,” there are 50 who
take their knowledge of English to one or other
country where it is spoken. Apart from which
important consideration, Russians are just now
thought of in Germany as blawsted foreigners,
whom Frenchmen effusively slobber over, a touch
of national insanity that tends to make-the Ger-
mans and British more of one kin than usual.

A Bulletin man had a horrid dream the
other night. He dreamt that an attorney was con-
victed of conspiracy and sentenced to some term
or other in gaol—he couldn’t catch the exact
sentence, for the end of the Judge’s wig had got
into his mouth, and made his utterance thick and
indistinct. Then the Judge took the end of his
wig out of his mouth and told the attorney that
he w r as a misguided young man, with a great com-
mand of fine language, yet, at the worst, he had
only conspin d to prove a guilty person innocent,
whereas another attorney (whose name The
Bulletin didn't catch distinctly, for the Judge’s
wig got into his mouth again) had committed the
much worse sin of conspiring to prove an innocent
person guilty ; and he hadn’t added pei jury to his
other sms, while the other man had ; and he was
young, just like the other man ; and, as the other
man had been released under the First Offenders’
Act, he couldn’t do less than release this attorney
also. He then went on to add that the second
attorney didn’t seem to have any influential rela-
tive-, like the other man, but that didn’t signify
in the-least, as the law cared nothing about influ-
ential relatives. Then the end of his wig got into
his mouth again and choked his utterance, and,
before he had done picking the end of it out of his
teeth, The Bulletin man awoke.

OF THE
The apparently-unfounded ground of op-
position to the national purchase of artist Tur-
ner's house at Chelsea is that it was a brothel.
Sample of impregnably conclusive evi-
dence given by a Sydney solicitor in rebuttal of a
sinister allegation : “I only took £1 from her to
'o on with the case, and I should not have been
satisfied with that if I had known she had <£C4 in
the bank ” :
Breathes there a shark with soul so dead,
A lawyer-shark who heard this said.
Who could have thought the matter minced,
And heard his brother, unconvinced ?
If such there be, we pray you, mark
Him well. He’s fit for Cullan Park.
11l a shell recently found by a N. Queens-
land pearler were discovered twin pearls, joined
together—one of a silvery hue, the other jet-
black. The twins were of good lustre and nicely-
rounded form except at the join. The singular
combination is, it is believed, unprecedented. The
owner actually wanted, at first, to separate them.

lionaire and his son were at breakfast in a palatial
’Frisco hotel, and the young man was very rude
to the waiter. “Don’t speak like that to the
waiter,’’ said the old man, reprovingly; “he
might spit in our food before bringing it to us.”

Since Then .
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I met Jack Ellis in town to-day—
Jack Ellis, my old mate, Jack-
Ten years ago, from the Caatlereagh,
We carried our swags together away
To the Never-Again, Out Back.
But times have altered since those old days,
And the times have changed the men.
Ah,-well 1 there’s little to blame or praise—
Jack Ellis and I have tramped long ways
On different tracks since then,
His hat was battered, his coat was green,
The toes of his boots were through,
But the pride was his I It was I felt mean—
I wished my collar was not so clean,
And the clothes I wore so new.
He saw me first, and ho knew ’twas I
The holiday swell he met.
Why have we no faith in each other ? Ah, w r hy ?
For he made as though he would pass me by,
He thought that I might forget.
Ho ought to have known me better than that,
By the tracks we tramped far out—
The sweltering scrub and the blazing flat,
When the heat came down through each old felt
hat
In the hell-born western drought;
The cheques we made and the shanty sprees,
The camps in the great blind scrub,
The long wet tramps when the plains were seas
And the oracles worked in days like these
For rum and tobacco and grub.
Could I forget how we struck “ the same
Old tale ” in the nearer West,
When the first great test of our friendship came
But—well, thtre’s little to praise or blame
If our mateship stood the test.
“ Heads !” he laughed (but his face was stern)—-
“ Tails 1” and a friendly oath ;
We loved her fair, we had much to learn,
And each was stabbed to the heart in turn
By the girl who —loved us both.
Or the last day lost on the lignum plain,
When I staggered, half-blind, half-dead
With a burning throat and a tortured brain,
And the tank when we came to the track again
Was seventeen miles ahead.
Then life seemed finished—then death began
As down in the dust I sank,
But he stuck to his mate as a bushman can
Till I heard him saying, “ Bear up, old man ! ’
In the shade by the mulga tank.
He took my hand in a distant way
(I thought how we parted last),
And we seemed like men who have nought to say
And who meet —“Good-day,” and who part
“ Good-day,”
Who never have shared the past.
I asked him in for a drink with me
Jack Ellis—my old mate, Jack —
But his manner no longer was careless and free,
He followed, but not with the grin that he
Woie always in days Out Back.
I tried to live in the past once more—
Or the present and past combine,
But the days between I could not ignore—
I couldn’t help notice the clothes ho wore
And ho couldn’t bub notice mine.
Ho placed bis glass on the polished bar
And ho wouldn’t fill up again ;
For be is prouder than most men are
Jack Ellis and I have tramped too far
On different tracks “since then.”
He said that he bad a mate to meet,
And “ I’ll see you again,” said ho,
And ho hurried away through the crowded street,
And the rattle of ’buses and scrape of feet
Seemed suddenly loud to me.
And I almost wished that the time were come
When less will be left to Fate—
When boys will start on the track from home
With equal chances, and no old chum
Have more or less than his mate.
H.L.

“ There is not one man in 7500 that can
tell what a pictured face is intended to express.
There is not one man in 500 that can go into a
court-room and be sure that be will not mistake
some harmless innocent of a juryman for the
black-hearted assassin on trial. Yet such people
talk of ‘ character ’ and presume to interpret ‘ ex-
pression ’ in pictures.”— Mark Twain in “Inno-
cents Abroad.”
A correspondent:—“N.S.W, Customs
landing waiters (the most responsible officers in
the department, for they see the goods, while
others see only papers which profess to relate to
the goods) receive 2s. an hour overtime. But they
must not earn more than .£1 per night, though
they remain on duty from 5 p.m. till 8 a.m.,
which brings the 2s. down to Is. 4d. Wharf-
laborers receive Is. 6d. an hour for night-work.”
Prof. Jenks in his “ History of the Austra-
lasian Colonies,” just published, ascribes the
beastly Austwalian prejudice against Ah Sin and
00. to the opium war of 1841, when a rumour that
the Ohows meant bo poison their tea chests struck
terror to the soul of this continent. Jenks must
be rather previous. Within the past eight years,
or so, Melbourne Argus declared that anci-Chow
sentiment was a novelty introduced by a “dis-
reputable Sydney newspaper,” and forming its
“sole stock in trade.” The Bulletin was the
d. S. n. in question.
“ The square,” exclaimed, a pint - pot
philosopher the other day, “is entirely an artificial
conceit due to the limitations of the human mind.
Search all through Nature and nothing square or
really straight can be found. Everything is the
section of a circle, or the combination of sections
of circles. Yet man will persist in having things
about him that are square ; in worrying his neigh-
bors into being square; and in attempting to be
square himself. Look, for instance, at Melbourne I
The streets are laid out in squares, and what is
the result? Why, simply, that through constantly
turning corners the citizens have acquired habits
of motion like the knight in chess. They can’t go
straight forward if they try. There is only one
cure for the Southern Sodom : it must be
demolished and rebuilt in crescents.”
Echoes from Drought-Land.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Players for rain are only effectual in the
coastal districts. There is no Providence “out-
back.” Away in the western districts of N.S.
Wales and Queensland the drought is still
supreme.
* « •
The river Darling is a visible sign of the
future’s gloomy outlook. She creeps over her
shallows at funeral pace, while here and there
small soakage-springs leak through the high
hanks in noble hut feeble effort to assist their
once powerful mistress. The creeks that fed the
river have long since ceased moaning, and all the
tanks are dry. Every pot-hole is lined with car-
cases. Crows, hawks and dingoes feast gloriously;
and the wandering sundowner, who seldom washes,
is now substantially excused. The ghastly country
west of the “ gutter ” is once more a credit to its
title, “Land of Droughts.”
• * *
One class of western people never fully
realise a calamity until it is over. These folk still
close the other eye to look skywards, and in grave,
mysterious accents whisper, “ It’s going to be a
drought.” Another class —the far-seeing lot— in-
stead of sinking tanks are building dams round
their half-acre cultivation paddocks, by way of pre-
paration for the flood, which they already swear
will follow the drought.
• • •
Shearing has begun later than usual, and
is carried on under extreme difficulties —the sheep
being too weak to travel. It is pitiful to watch
the starving creatures stagger up to the sheds.
To lie down means certain death to most of them,
as few would be able to rise again. The poor
animals seem to know this, and try their utmost
to keep on their legs by leaning against the gates
and fences. Most large holdings will be compelled
to shear part of their sheep under bough sheds in
the paddocks. Unionists, “scabs” and black-
legs are to-day working peacefully side by side,
forgetful of last year’s trouble. Strikes are out
of the question this season : neither side can afford
them. Nature, in her own decisive way, has
silenced “ Slingem, Orabbit and Co.” and their
Bth clause. The clip is of but medium quality
and poor in quantity, the staple being short. The
wool lacks weight and life — i.e., yolk ; cause, want
of green feed.

in the monotony of N.S.W, backblocks existence.
A commercial traveller commented upon the
quietude of a Darling River township. “ Yer

strapping young native of 20 years. “ My wurd !
it wuz lively here then !” “ Ah ! what was on —a
Wedding?” “No-o-00, it wasn’t no wedding, it

Know the time when everybody is blind drunk.”

Back-country people make a kind and generous

Swag-carrying women are common on tJie
lonely Western roads, and hereby hangs a story.
A Sydney youth in depressed circumstances
dressed up his best girl in a loud suit of Ms own
clothes and faced with her the P.U. office in search
of work. Both got a job at a shearing shed one
the Q. border. Provided with a conditional rail-
way-pass they trained it to Bourke, and thence
walked to their destination —-200 miles off. Bare ;
footed, girlish-looking youths are roaming about
the backblocks in numbers, nowadays, and the
romantic pair passed unnoticed. Arrived at the
shed the young man went shearing and his
girl mate “ picking - up.” The nice-looking
“ picker-up ” soon became the favorite of all
hands. Nobody even suspected her sex until
“cutting-out” day. It’s a beastly but common
practice amongst shearers at “ cut-out time ’ to
tear the clothes off each other as soon as the last
sheep has been shorn. On that eventful day
several shearers rushed the pretty rouseabout,
and !

While in the cities legislators, writers,
reformers and agitators exhaust themselves iff**
trying to dissuade people from dealing with alien
races, the Chinaman, the Afghan, and other
“colored gentlemen” are grinning up their
sleeves as they slowly gain a stronger foothold in
the back country. A N.W. settler is reported to;
have recently received >£3oo cash from a colored
man for the hand of his daughter. She married
the black intruder, and will probably live happy
with him —until the first black-skinned baby makes-
its appearance.
# • #
At the commencement of shearing at a
Darling River shed four men put up for shearers’
cook—three Europeans and an American nigger.
The darkie was elected by a largo majority, and
the white men slipped the usual curse and walked.
The blackfellovv turned out a duffer, and was
duly sacked—to be replaced by a Chinaman who
was elected by the same majority. Only one
white—a Frenchman —was present on this
occasion, and was objected to because he had no
Union-ticket. (Chinamen are under any circum-
stances denied admission to the Union). Poor
Frerichy, a brand new-chura, and, judging by his
ragged appearance, very hard-up, implored work
in weird accents : “ I vill accept une tickee si je
regois de work.” In vain; he had to go and
“ catchoe’nother piecee box.”

[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“ Hide—Halt ! Make much of your
horses ! ’’
“ Very good that leading file, Price right.
Very well ridden ! ”
“ But as for you, Price left, you hump backed,
dog-eared defaulter, you ride like a dam tailor. A
bag of bran has a better seat on a horse ! I’ll wake
you up yet, curse you, you blanky object 1 Take
punishment riding-school in the evenings until
further orders ! ”
And the speaker crossed the manege, fumbled
with the culprit’s stirrup-iron, and, under pre-
tence of putting his leg into position, slyly chalked
a cross on the sole of his boot.
“Ride, attention ! Walk ! March ! T-r-r-rot!
I’ll bump hell out of you, Price left ! ”
It was only old Crutch, riding-master 22nd
Hussars, drilling a morning troop ride at Benares,
Bengal, and he was a riding-master of trie antique
pattern, and had rather more than ten inches ot
whisky inside him, though it was not yet 6 o’clock.
Yet he voiced, in uncouth and ungodly manner, a
regimental sentiment. For Price “ left,” nearly
the sloppiest Hussar and quite the worst rider in
the corps, was trying enough to make a cherubim,
let alone Crutch, blaspheme.
a * •
The Prices were twins, called respectively
“right” and “left” by the authorities, and
“right” and “wrong” by their comrades, with
the wild idea of distinguisiiing one from other.
Physically, they were literally alike as two peas,
so that no man might tell which was which when
they were apart. Only a very few professed to be
able to tell when the brothers were together. The
personal resemblance was otherwise complicated.
Price “right” was named John, the other
Jonathan. The regimental number of John was
2212, of Jonathan 2221; they enlisted at same
hour and place, were posted to same troop (nothing
could be done with either when the experiment of
separating them was tried); they slept next cot to
each other, wore one-another’s clothes, boots, and
accoutrements indifferently, fought together like
jungle-cats, and turned, like devils of the Rechna,
on any that interfered with both or either. They
were never long apart, though John was normally
sober, and Jonathan abnormally drunken. Both
were shy, sly, and secretive little men, and both
loved the same girl. She, indeed, claimed to be
able to tell Jonathan from John. But even girls
of seventeen are not omniscient.
* # •
The facts that John was sober, a clean,
smart soldier and a good rider, while Jonathan
was in these respects his antithesis, should, it may
b • imagined, have afforded ample means of iden-
tificition. They didn’t. For instance, Crutch
frequently suspected that the Price who presented
himself for punishment riding was not the real
offender, but his virtuous brother making vicarious
atonement for him. Hence Crutch’s device of
chalking Jonathan’s boots. “And that,” as the
rough riding sergeant p anted out to his superior,
“considering as that reptile Jonathan as often as
not wears John’s boots, don’t count for much.”
Then again, when half the troop were certain that
the person lying drunk and asleep at watchsetting
on Jonathan’s cot was Jonathan, the other half
were as furiously cocksure that John must have
gone on a rare razzle-dazzle, and that the indi-
vidual lying on John’s charpoy was Jonathan
suffering a spasm of rare sobriety. Nor was it
judicious on such occasions to seek elucidation of
tbe Bob ® r m , a «* T he twins were techy as scor-
pions, brooking no intermeddling, and were
credited with being ready to reply to ridicule or
impertinent inquisitiveness with a Snider bullet,
# # #
Sergeant Grice’s only daughter, Martha —-
pink, pretty, fair-haired, blue-eyed, and chaste,
though born and bred in Indian military canton-
ments—was desperately fond of one of the Prices
—naturally, the bad one. John, in his cold, shy
way, was desperately in love with Martha. But
she would have none of him. “Yes,” she would
say, when old Grice grumbled, “John may be a
first chop barrack-room soldier ; but Jonathan’s a
wan, if he is a bit sloppy. An’if it come to ser-
vice, you’d see, father, who’s the best soldier.”
Martha flattered herself that she could always,
and by j ns tinct, distinguish between the twins,
until a sudden suspicion seized her that she had
more than once been kisBed by and i, ad kissed
J obn in mistake for his brother. She dared not
satisfy her doubts by asking Jonathan where he
j, ad be en on certain occasions, lest the twins
should argue with their carbines. So she tattooed
a small circular mark on the back of her hand,
and then a ; m il ar l y decorated Jonathan- 11 so’s we
can be both alike ,” B he explained. This arrange-
ment would have been decisive had not John
appeared a few hours after with an identical mark.
Then Martha was certain John had personated
his brother, and her faint dislike turned to pro-
nounced loathing. Also to fear. “I think I’d
kill myself if that John were to kiss me,” she
remarked to her bosom friend. “ I know I would
if I had to marry the beast,” she added. Assisted
by her military knowledge, Martha invented a
more cunning test. When Jonathan came to woo
she would challenge: “Who comes there?” To
which he was supposed to reply—“ Rounds ! ”
She—“ What rounds?” He—“ Grand rounds.”
Then she would demand that he “stand and give
the countersign.”
Now the word, which was often changed and
always whispered, was known only to these two.
Thus was John baffled and moved to a great envy
and hate.
* * *
There came a hell-hot night when the

and appalling outcry in the bungalow. * The grim

muttering. “ Some of those beggars’ve been ’avin’

ar f their complaint ! ” Aloud, he bellowed, “If

“ What yer gassin’ about, corporal ? It’saman
"with cholera—one of the Prices.”
And that was true. Between the cots of the
twins lay one writhing and bounding with the
devilish contortions of cholera, the cramps run-
ning up and down his limbs like big balls of
quicksilver beneath the skin. The man was
already changing color.
“ Which of you is it ? ” said the corporal to the
Price kneeling by the side of his stri ken brother,
“ Which ?” stammered the other, his teeth chat-
tering even in that torrid atmosphere. “Which?
Why, John, of course, who else?”
“I don’t know nothing about ‘of course,’”
growled the corporal. “I should have taken you
to be John.”
Price turned savagely on him, “Are you going
to pay out slack jaw the whole blarsted night, or
are you going to send my brother to hospital,
man ? ”
But a man had gone, hot foot, to the hospital,
and the dhooli bearers were even then chattering,
and clattering their dhooli poles, in the verandah.
• * #
They carried the sick man away. His
brother watched him eagerly, jealously, until, in
the gray of morn, when the jackals had ceased
howling and the pariah dogs and other vermin of
the dark had sought foul kenneling, the dying
man raised himself, all racked, twisted, and de-
faced by the infernal torments of the night.
“Martha!” he called, with voice as terrible
and ai changed as his countenance.
Then, and more loudly, “ Grand Rounds ! ”
A pause—“ The countersign ?” he gasped.
Then he moaned a word unheard by any save
his brother.
But the real countersign was—Death, which
smote him.
* • #
Next morning at orderly-room the colonel
read the hospital report and swore. “Damn it,
John Price dead ! Might have taken the other.
Always the way, by Jove ! ”
But he sent for the survivor and spoke to him
not unkindly : “Look here, Price, turn over a
new leaf. I’ll give you a chance. I’ll send you
to Oawnpore to the left wing, where they don’t
know you. If you go on all right for six months
I’ll give you the lance-stripe and—and, yes, I will,
leave to marry.”
There was a strange light in the man’s eyes as
he stammered thanks, saluted, and filed out.
Price saw Martha, for the first time since his
brother’s death, that night. But only for a
minute or so. Brief as was the interview, she
challenged him and he answered after the usual
fashion. But he was vexed. “ You needn’t have
done that,” he said, with a catch in his voice,
“John’s dead enough, and buried.” Then he
kissed her and hastened away, being in orders for
that night’s train to Oawnpore.
Martha for many days went about with a scared
and perplexed face. Yet, when Price wrote regu-
larly, and when, later, the adjutant rode out of
his way to the patcheri to tell the girl, who was a
regimental pet, that Price was doing well at
Oawnpore—“getting as good a rider as his brother
John was, and has regularly put the peg in ;
never tastes,” was the report—she seemed to
become re-assured.
* * *
Seven months after Price and Martha
were married, “with leave.” The following day
she was drawn, dripping and dead, out of the
Ganges.
* # •
Nor could satisfactory explanation be ob-
tained from the bridegroom, because, three hours
before they found his wife, he had borrowed a
carbine and, with a bullet therefrom, removed a
large portion of his head.
# # #
“ How shall I enter Price's suicide in the
records, sir ? Shall I describe him as Jonathan or
John?” enquired the orderly-room clerk of the
adjutant.
“Ay, indeed ! ” replied that officer, “Jonathan
or John —Which ? ”

The other day a Melb. man, arrested for
smashing .£8 worth of plate-glass in a local
restaurant, pleaded that there were cockroaches in
the soup. Pined £15 or two months. Now that

Apropos Gov. Buxton and his indirect
connection with the Accursed Drink, one of tin-
first functions he has been invited to attend in
Adelaide is a big Vf.M.C.A. and W.C.T.II. de-
monstration, which will be a rallying ground for
all the pious teetotallers in the sacred city.
In the hottest jjart of last summera Syd-
ney bank official was sent out to a far-west town
where dust and flies are the only features o:
interest. There were two branch banks in the
town, and the two managers met him at the
station, and, having introduced themselves and
shaken hands, they solemnly made the stranger
free of their bath-rooms during his stay. Ha,
might come round at any hour of the day or even-
ing, each manager said, and if he hadn’t time to
atop and have a chat and a drink he was simply
to send the girl for a clean towel and go straight
to the bath-room, if he could stop and have a
drink so much the better, but anyhow he was to
go to the bath-room. Then the Sydney man went
to his hotel in a surprised frame of mind, but
when he found that the average temperature was
100° or so, and that the two bankers possessed the
only bath-rooms in the town, the real beauty of
this hospitality became apparent. So during his
stay he had a bath with one banker in the morn-
ing, and bathed with the other in the after-
noon, and occasionally he had a bath in the even"
ing. One of the bankers had a room with a hole
in the floor to allow the water to escape, and you
stood around and emptied the bucket over yourself
till you were tired ; at this place the servant girl
used to sling the towel in through the fan-light, and
the banker used to call though the keyhole inviting'
the visitor to drop in when fie was dressed and
have some beer. At the other place there was a
tub to stand in while you poured the water over
yourself, and the daughter of the house threw the
towel over the partition, and some whisky and
soda was left on the table outside the door. In*
this place the soap was kept in a pie-dish, while in
the other it lay about on the floor. The daughter
was 16 years old and as pretty as a picture, and
she would sing snatches of opera in a fresh young
voice as she hove the towel over the partition, and
then the visitor would stand in the tub with the'
bucket in his hand and indulge in poetic reverie.
Altogether, he says that the happi. st hours of his
life were spent in that bath room.

(Published at Sydney, N.S.W.)
The Unique Weekly— Australia's Nonpareil
Illustrated Newspaper,
“The Bulletl n,”
Premier Australian Literary Journal,
Is the only paper which has a consistent and d is
tinctive Australian National Policy*
The Bulletin favours—
A Republican Form of Government.
One Person, one Vote.
Complete Secularisation and Freedom of State
-Education.
Reform of the Criminal Code and Prison
System.
A United Australia and Protection against
the World.
Australia for the Australians. —The cheap
Chinaman, the cheap nigger, and the
cheap European pauper to be absolutely
excluded.
A State Bank, the issue of bank-notes to be a
State monopoly.
The direct election of Ministers by Parlia-
ment, instead of Party Government or
rather Government by Contradiction.
A new Parliamentary System one House to
be elected by constituencies as at present;
the other to be chosen by the whole

A Universal System of Compulsory Life
The entire Abolition of the Private Owner-
ship of Land.
The Referendum.
The Abolition of Titles of so-called “nobility.
The public eye rejects as uninteresting more
than half of what appears in the newspapers of
the day. It is only the remainder that is found
in The Bulletin.
The Bulletin is an aggressive Democratic
paper which strives to exclude from its artisti-
cally-condensed columns all matter that is nob
of general human interest. The Bulletin was
started 13 years ago with no capital but brains,
and has become the representative Australian
journal, because it possesses a vitality lacked
by the countless newspaper-ventures in which
Australian capitalists have unavailingly spent
arge fortunes.
The Bulletin’s conductors claim that every
progressive paper on the Australian continent has
more or less endeavoured to profit by its example
and follow its lines. The result of its powerful
influence and its unprecedented literary and corn,
mercial success can at once be seen by comparing
the met hods pursued by the Australian daily and
weekly press prior to and immediately after th
first issue of The Bulletin.
From the date of The Bulletin’s inaugural
publication its success was assured. It had at
once a dozen more or less servile imitators, many
of which even took, so far as was possible, its
name. They are now all dead, while The Bul-
letin Continues the one phenomenal success in
Australian journalism, the one completely non-
local and distinctly universal Australasian news-
paper.
The Bulletin proprietary have spent some
thousands of pounds in fitting their buildings
with the latest appliances for high-class printing
and the production of illustrations, and they have
now issued The Bulletin in an entirely new
form —printed with new type of improved legi-
bility on the finest paper, and adorned with
sketches from the hands of the ablest and best-
paid newspaper artists Australia has ever seen.
The Bulletin is the only well-established Aus-
tralian journal which, throughout its career, has
been consistently Radical. It points with pride
to the fact that for years it stood alone among
secular newspapers in advocating the Irish
National Cause, and that it was the only Aus-
tralian journal which denounced the Soudan
Expedition from its inception.
The Bulletin is the beacon-fire of national
progress: it is the forceful summons to 'the
crusade against the monopolies in land, wealth,
power, and privilege—the direct cause of half the
misery which now afflicts British humanity.
Against the chums of avaricious clergy who claim
to monopolise salvation, aerainst the dark despotism
of grasping plutocrats, and against the exercise of
any human authority which has not been granted
by the people over whom it is exercised, The
Bulletin will fearlessly and ruthlessly contend.
The Bulletin, unlike the ordinary weekly
journal, is not loaded with a weight of matter
transferred from .the columns of a daily paper
issued from the same office. The Bulletin is
not connected with any other paper, and the
matter it contains is all specially prepared by its
own unequalled staff.
The Bulletin is the journal which, of all Aus-
tralian journals, is most frequently quoted in Eng-
land, America, and the colonies.
The Bulletin, which is the only paper sold
by runners in the streets of every Australasian
capital, is the most widely-circulated of all papers
published south of the equator, and is the only
Australasian publication which enjoys a popu-
larity unhampered by the political boundaries
that so closely restrict other journals to the
in which they are printed.
The Bulletin’s red cover is equally familiar
to the bushman of the Far North, the stockman
of Central Australia, the pearl-sheller of Torres
Straits, and the digger in the Maoriland
Ranges. A paper which is at once the most
popular city publication and the organ of the
intelligent bushman must indeed be broadly based.
Perhaps the strongest proof of The Bulletin’s
popularity is found in the unchallengeable fact—
easily asceftainable from any postal official—that,
of all journals issued in Australasia, The Bul-
letin is the one most frequently remailed from
one reader to another.
The Bulletin is going ahead faster than and
other paper, because its vast mass of original and
specially-written reading matter is the brightest
and best, its illustrations the cleverest and most
artistic, and its policy the boldest and most
straightforward ever submitted to the Australian
public.
SPECIAL NOTICE.
Subscription (payable strictly in advance), Six
Shillings and Sixpence per Quarter, or One Pound
per Annum.
Business communications to he addressed to
William Macleod, Managing Director, to whom
all remittances are payable. Editorial communi-
cations to be addressed to J. F. Archibald.
